Sales by Minors, Slaves, and Conditions of the Contracting Party
Capacity of minors and slaves in sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on sales by minors and slaves, and identify which conditions are not required for a valid sale contract in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
A minor's sale is valid but suspended; a slave's capacity mirrors a minor's; maturity, freedom, and Islam are not conditions.

This lesson addresses the capacity of minors and slaves in sale contracts. According to the Hanafi school, a minor's sale is not void but rather suspended, awaiting the guardian's permission. Similarly, a slave's capacity is analogous. Additionally, the passage clarifies that maturity, freedom, Islam, speech, and consciousness are not conditions for the validity of a sale—meaning a non-Muslim, a slave, or a mute can contract, provided they have understanding.

Guided Path
1
Understand the minor's ruling
A minor's sale or purchase is valid (sahih) but suspended (mawquf) on the guardian's permission if it concerns the minor's own property.
Suspended means it is not binding until guardian approves.
2
Minor purchasing for another
If the minor buys for another person, the contract is binding on that person with no liability on the minor.
The minor acts as a messenger.
3
Apply to the slave
A slave's capacity in sale is identical to the minor's: valid but suspended if for himself, binding without liability if for another.
The slave is like the minor in both types.
4
Conditions not required
Maturity (bulugh), freedom ('itq), Islam, speech (nutq), and consciousness (sahw) are not conditions for a valid sale contract.
These affect enforceability, not validity per se.
5
Reconcile consciousness
The statement that consciousness is not a condition is reconciled by understanding that minimal awareness suffices; a contract by someone completely unconscious is void by consensus.
The passage likely means 'wakefulness' in a minimal sense.

Source Text

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
6 min
Conditions of Offer and Acceptance in a Sale
Conditions of Offer and Acceptance in a Sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid offer and acceptance in a Hanafi sale and identify when a deviation still leads to a valid contract.

Core Idea
A sale requires offer and acceptance to match exactly and occur in one session; deviations invalidate unless exceptions apply.

The contract of sale is built upon offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul). However, not every exchange of words creates a binding contract. In Hanafi fiqh, strict rules govern how these two elements must align. This lesson unpacks the conditions of correspondence (muwafaqa) and unity of session (majlis), along with important exceptions that arise in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main rule of correspondence
The acceptance must exactly match the offer in subject matter and price. If the buyer accepts a different item, part of it, or a different price, the contract is void due to splitting the transaction (tafarruq al-safqah).
2
Recognize the exception for pre-emption
In pre-emption (shuf'ah), the pre-emptor may take only part of the property (e.g., real estate from a mixed sale of slave and land) even if it splits the transaction against the seller.
This is a special concession; not applicable in regular sales.
3
Identify the exception for increase/decrease in price
If the seller offers at a price and the buyer accepts for less (or vice versa), the contract can still be valid if the other party agrees to the new price in the same session. The change is treated as a new offer and acceptance.
The key is that the counter-proposal and acceptance must occur in the same session.
4
Understand the requirement of past tense
If the contract is verbal, it must be expressed in the past tense (e.g., 'I sold' and 'I bought'). Present or future tense does not conclude the contract.
This is a formal requirement; writing has its own rules.
5
Confirm the necessity of a single session
The offer and acceptance must take place in the same sitting (majlis). If they occur in separate sessions, the contract does not conclude, because the session is considered a condition of place.
The Hanafi school does not recognize an option of the session (khayar al-majlis) unlike the Hanbalis; once the session ends, the contract is final.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما شرط العقد بيع فموافقة القبول للإيجاب بأن يقبل المشتري ما أوجبه البائع بما أوجبه فإن خالفه بأن قبل غير ما أوجبه أو بعض ما أوجبه أو بغير ما أوجبه أو ببعض ما أوجبه لم ينعقد لتفرق الصفقة، وأنه لا يجوز إلا في الشفعة بأن باع عبداً وعقاراً فطلب الشفيع أخذ العقار وحده فله ذلك وإن تفرقت الصفقة على البائع كما في الفتاوي الولوالجية من الشفعة وستأتي تفاريعه إلا فيما إذا كان الإيجاب من المشتري فقبل البائع بأنقص من الثمن أو كان من البائع فقبل المشتري بأزيد انعقد، فإن قبل البائع الزيادة في المجلس جازت كما في التتارخانية. وفي الآلة أن اتحاد تكون بلفظ الماضي إن عقد بالقول ؛ كذا في البدائع وأما شرط مكانه فواحد وهو المجلس بأن كان الإيجاب والقبول في مجلس واحد، فإن اختلف لم ينعقد. فانعقد.
As for the condition of the contract of sale, it is that the acceptance corresponds to the offer: the buyer accepts what the seller offered with what he offered. If he deviates by accepting other than what he offered, or part of what he offered, or with other than what he offered, or with part of what he offered, it does not conclude due to the splitting of the transaction. And it is not permissible except in pre-emption: if he sold a slave and real estate, and the pre-emptor demands to take only the real estate, he has that right even if the transaction splits against the seller, as in al-Fatawa al-Walwaljiyyah on pre-emption, and its details will follow. Except when the offer is from the buyer, then the seller accepts for less than the price, or from the seller, then the buyer accepts for more, it concludes. If the seller accepts the increase in the session, it is permissible, as in al-Tatar Khaniyyah. Regarding the instrument, it must be in the past tense if concluded verbally; thus in al-Bada'i. As for the condition of place, it is one: the session, where the offer and acceptance are in one session; if they differ, it does not conclude. Then it concludes.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage explains the essential conditions of the contract (shart al-'aqd) in a sale: the acceptance must match the offer exactly in subject matter and price. If the buyer accepts a different item, part of it, or a different price, the contract is invalid because the transaction would be split (tafarruq al-safqah). An exception is pre-emption (shuf'ah) where the pre-emptor may take only part of the property. Another exception is when the buyer offers and the seller accepts for less, or the seller offers and the buyer accepts for more; these are valid because the increase is considered a new offer accepted in the same session. The text also requires that the contract be expressed in past tense (madi) if done verbally, as per al-Bada'i. Lastly, the offer and acceptance must occur in the same sitting (majlis); if they are in different sessions, the contract does not conclude. This is the Zahir al-Riwayah position.

Key Terms
موافقة القبول للإيجاب (muwafaqat al-qabul lil-ijab): The condition that acceptance must correspond exactly to the offer in subject, price, and terms.
تفرق الصفقة (tafarruq al-safqah): Splitting the single transaction into parts, which invalidates the contract.
المجلس (al-majlis): The sitting or session in which the contract is made; offer and acceptance must be in the same majlis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if a buyer accepts a price lower than what the seller offered?
A) The contract is valid at the lower price.
B) The contract is void because acceptance does not match the offer.
C) The contract is valid if the seller agrees in the same session.
D) The contract is suspended.

Key Takeaway
A valid sale requires the acceptance to match the offer exactly in subject and price and to occur in the same session; deviations lead to invalidity unless the other party accepts the change in that same session.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

6 min
The Five Conditions of the Object of Sale
Opening statement of conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five conditions of the object of sale and identify which condition is missing in a given scenario.

Core Idea
The object must be existent, valuable, owned, owned by seller, and deliverable; absence voids the sale.

The Hanafi school lays down precise conditions for what can be sold. If any condition is missing, the sale is invalid. This lesson introduces these five essential conditions.

Guided Path
1
List the five conditions
Recall the five conditions from the passage: existence, being valuable property, owned in itself, owned by the seller, capable of delivery.
Memorise them in this order.
2
Define each condition
Existence: the object must be present at the time of contract. Valuable property: it must have legal commercial value. Owned: it must be owned by someone. Owned by seller: if selling for himself, he must be the owner. Capable of delivery: physically deliverable.
These definitions are crucial for application.
3
Apply to a scenario
Given a case like selling a stolen car, identify that the seller does not own it, so the sale is invalid.
Practice with the examples in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما شرائط المعقود عليه فأن يكون موجوداً مالاً متقوماً مملوكا في نفسه، وأن يكون ملك البائع فيما يبيعه لنفسه، وأن يكون مقدور التسليم فلم ينعقد بيع المعدوم وماله خطر. العدم كنتاج النتاج والحمل واللبن في الضرع والثمر والزرع قبل الظهور، والبزر في البطيخ، والنوى في التمر، واللحم في الشاة الحية والشحم والألية فيها وأكارعها ورأسها، والسجير في السمسم، وهذا الفص على أنه ياقوت فإذا هو زجاج، أو هذا الثوب الهروي فإذا هو مروي، أو هذا العبد فإذا هو جارية، أو دار على أن بناءها آجر فإذا هو لبن، أو ثوب على أنه مصبوغ بعصفر فإذا هو بزعفران، أو هو حنطة في جوالق فإذا هي دقيق، أو خبز، أو هذا الثوب القز فإذا لحمته من ملحم ولو كان سداه من قز، وصح لو كان عكسه مع الخيار إذا اللحمة . من كذا فإذا الظهارة من غير المعين بخلاف ما إذا كانت البطانة من غير المعين فإنه ينعقد مع الخيار. ومما تسامحوا فيه وأخرجوه عن هذه القاعدة ما في القنية : الأشياء التي تؤخذ من العادة من غير بيع كالعدس والملح والزيت ونحوها ثم اشتراها البياع على وجه الخرج كما هو صح اهـ. فيجوز بيع المعدوم هنا. ولم ينعقد بيع ما ليس بمال متقوم كبيع الحر والمدبر المطلق وأم الولد والمكاتب ومعتق البعض وأولادهم إلا ولد المكاتب المشتري في كتابته والميتة والدم وذبيحة المجوسي والمرتد والمشرك والصبي الذي لا يعقل والمجنون ومذبوح صيد المحرم، سواء كان من الحل أو الحرم ومذبوح صيد الحرم وصيد المحرم إلا بيع و وكيله ، وجلد الميتة قبل الدبغ وجلد الخنزير مطلقاً وعظمه وشعره وعصبه على الصحيح كشعر الآدمي وعظمه، وفي عظم الكلب روايتان. ولم ينعقد بيع الخمر والخنزير في حق المسلم، وأما في حق الذمي فينعقد ولكن اختلفوا في كونه مباحاً له أو محرماً والصحيح الثاني كما في البدائع لكونهم يتمولونها . وإن تبايعا ثم أسلم أحدهما قبل القبض انفسخ البيع، ولو تقارضا ثم أسلم المقرض فلا شيء له من الخمر، وإن أسلم المستقرض كان عليه القيمة في رواية، وفي أخرى كالأول. ولم ينعقد بيع النحل ودود القز إلا تبعاً، ولا بيع العذرة الخالصة بخلاف السرقين والمخلوطة بتراب، وكذا بيع آلات الملاهي عندهما خلافاً للإمام، ولم ينعقد بيع الملاقيح والمضامين وعسب الفحل ولبن المرأة، وفي التلويح : المتقوم ما يجب ابقاؤه بعينه الفرع وللفرع الآتي عن القنية أيضاً وهو بيع البراءات وذكره لكلام المؤلف أقول: الظاهر أن ما في القنية ضعيف لاتفاق كلمتهم على أن بيع المعدوم لا يصح وكذا غير المملوك، وما المانع من أن يكون المأخوذ من العدس ونحوه بيعاً بالتعاطي ولا يحتاج في مثله إلى بيان الثمن لأنه معلوم كما سيأتي وحظ الإمام لا يملك قبل القبض فأني يصح بيعه؟ وكن على ذكر مما قاله ابن وهبان في كتاب الشرب ما في القنية إذا كان مخالفاً للقواعد لا التفات إليه ما لم يعضه نقل .اهـ. قال الحموي في كون المأخوذ من العدس ونحوه بيعاً بالتعاطي وأنه لا يحتاج في مثله إلى بيان الثمن نظر، لأن أثمان هذه تختلف فيفضي إلى المنازعة .اهـ. وأنت خبير بأن ما في النهر مبني على العلم به فحينئذ يقال إن كان معلوماً.
As for the conditions of the object of sale: it must be existing, valuable property, owned in itself, owned by the seller if he sells for himself, and capable of delivery. So the sale of a non-existent thing or something with risk does not conclude. Non-existence includes: the offspring of offspring, the fetus, milk in the udder, fruit and crops before appearance, seeds in watermelon, pits in dates, meat on a live sheep, fat, tail, legs, head; sesame oil in sesame; this gem supposed to be ruby but it is glass; this Herawi cloth but it is Marwi; this slave but it is a female slave; a house thought to be brick but it is adobe; a cloth thought dyed with safflower but it is saffron; wheat in a sack but it is flour or bread; this silk cloth with weft of mixed silk and wool, even if warp is pure silk; valid if opposite with option if the weft is from something else, then the outer is unspecified; unlike if the lining is unspecified, then it concludes with option. Among what they permitted and excepted from this rule is what is in al-Qunyah: things customarily taken without sale, like lentils, salt, oil, etc., then the vendor buys them back as outgoing; so it is correct, thus sale of non-existent is allowed here. And the sale of what is not valuable property does not conclude, like the sale of a free person, a mudabbar absolutely, umm walad, mukatib, part-free slave, and their children except the child of a mukatib who buys himself in his kitabah; carcass, blood, slaughter of Majusi, apostate, idolator, child who does not understand, insane person, sacrificial animal of a Muhrim whether from halal or haram, slaughter of game from Haram, and game of a Muhrim except sale and his agent; skin of carcass before tanning, pig skin absolutely, its bone, hair, sinew as the correct view, like human hair and bone; about dog bone two narrations. The sale of wine and pig is not valid for the Muslim; as for the dhimmi, it is valid but they differed whether permissible for him or prohibited, and the correct is the second as in al-Bada'i because they consider it property. If they trade then one becomes Muslim before possession, the sale is rescinded. If they exchange a loan then the lender becomes Muslim, he has nothing of the wine; if the borrower becomes Muslim, he must pay its value in one narration, in another like the first. The sale of bees and silkworms is not valid except as a follow-on; nor pure dung, unlike manure mixed with soil; likewise the sale of musical instruments according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) contrary to Abu Hanifa. The sale of unfertilized eggs, fetuses, stud fees, and woman's milk is not valid. In al-Talwih: valuable property is what must be preserved in specie. For the branch and the next branch from al-Qunyah also: the sale of exemptions (barra'at). And the author's statement: it appears that what is in al-Qunyah is weak due to their consensus that sale of non-existent is invalid, likewise unowned property. What prevents what is taken from lentils etc. from being a sale by mutual dealing (ta'ati) and not needing statement of price because it is known? But the share of the imam is not owned before possession, so how can its sale be valid? Keep in mind what Ibn Wahban said in Kitab al-Shurb: what is in al-Qunyah if contrary to the rules is not relied upon unless supported by transmission. Al-Hamawi said: regarding taking lentils etc. as sale by mutual dealing and not needing statement of price, it is disputable because prices vary, leading to disputes. And you are aware that what is in al-Nahr is based on knowledge; then it is said if it is known.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage details the conditions that the object of sale must satisfy: existence (wujud), being valuable property (mal mutaqawwim), being owned (mamluk), being owned by the seller (if selling for himself), and being capable of delivery (maqdur al-taslim). It then provides a long list of things whose sale is invalid because they are non-existent, not property, not owned, or not deliverable. Examples of non-existent things include unborn animals, milk in udder, fruits before they appear, etc. Also objects sold with an attribute that turns out to be false (e.g., ruby turns out to be glass) are invalid unless a condition of option is made. An exception is made for items customarily taken back by vendors like lentils, salt, oil—these are considered sellable even though technically non-existent at the time of purchase because it is a known practice. The passage then lists items that are not valuable property (ghayr mutaqawwim): free humans, certain categories of slaves, carcass, blood, the slaughter of apostates, etc. Wine and pig are not valid for Muslims but for dhimmis there is ikhtilaf; the correct view is that it is prohibited for them as well, but they own it as property. If one party converts to Islam before possession, the sale dissolves. Bees and silkworms are not sold alone but only as part of the hive. Pure dung is not sold, but manure mixed with soil is. Musical instruments are invalid according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, but Abu Hanifa allowed it. Finally, the text discusses a critique of al-Qunyah's exception, noting that many scholars consider it weak because it contradicts the rule that non-existent things cannot be sold, and the prices may be unknown, leading to disputes. Thus the relied-upon position is that such transactions must be valid sales by mutual dealing (ta'ati) only if the price is known.

Key Terms
المال المتقوم (al-mal al-mutaqawwim): Property that is legally recognized and has commercial value; excludes impure substances and free humans.
المعدوم (al-ma'dum): A non-existent item, such as an unborn animal or future fruit, whose sale is invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which condition is violated if someone sells a stolen laptop?
A) Existence
B) Valuable property
C) Owned by seller
D) Capable of delivery

Key Takeaway
The five conditions of the object of sale are existence, being valuable property, being owned, being owned by the seller (if selling for himself), and being deliverable; absence of any makes the sale void.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Invalid Sales Due to Non-Existence and Attribute Mistake
List of invalid sales due to non-existence and attribute mismatch with option rule and an exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which sales are invalid due to non-existence of the object or a mistake in its attribute, and explain the role of option (khiyar) in attribute mistakes.

Core Idea
A sale is void if the object is non-existent or if its attribute is different from what was specified, unless an option is stipulated.

The Hanafi school enumerates many examples of non-existent things (like unborn animals, future fruit) and cases where the object turns out to be different from described (e.g., glass instead of ruby). Understanding these helps avoid invalid contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
The sale of a non-existent thing (ma'dum) does not conclude. Also, if the attribute differs materially, the buyer has the option to annul.
The key is existence at contract time.
2
Work through examples of non-existence
List examples from the text: offspring of offspring, fetus, milk in udder, fruit before appearance, etc. Understand why each is non-existent.
Note that some things like seeds inside fruit are considered non-existent.
3
Analyze attribute mistakes
When the object is described with a specific attribute (e.g., ruby) but found to be different (glass), the sale is invalid unless an option (khiyar) was stipulated.
The option allows the buyer to accept or reject.
4
Examine the exception in al-Qunyah
An exception is made for items customarily taken back by vendors (like lentils, salt, oil). This is considered a valid sale of what seems non-existent but is actually a known custom.
Later scholars critique this exception.

آلات الملاهي عندهما خلافاً للإمام، ولم ينعقد بيع الملاقيح والمضامين وعسب الفحل ولبن المرأة، وفي التلويح : المتقوم ما يجب ابقاؤه بعينه الفرع وللفرع الآتي عن القنية أيضاً وهو بيع البراءات وذكره لكلام المأخوذ من العدس ونحوه بيعاً بالتعاطي وأنه لا يحتاج في مثله إلى بيان الثمن نظر، لأن أثمان هذه تختلف فيفضي إلى المنازعة .اهـ. وأنت خبير بأن ما في النهر مبني على العلم به فحينئذ يقال إن كان معلوماً.
As for the conditions of the object of sale: it must be existing, valuable property, owned in itself, owned by the seller if he sells for himself, and capable of delivery. So the sale of a non-existent thing or something with risk does not conclude. Non-existence includes: the offspring of offspring, the fetus, milk in the udder, fruit and crops before appearance, seeds in watermelon, pits in dates, meat on a live sheep, fat, tail, legs, head; sesame oil in sesame; this gem supposed to be ruby but it is glass; this Herawi cloth but it is Marwi; this slave but it is a female slave; a house thought to be brick but it is adobe; a cloth thought dyed with safflower but it is saffron; wheat in a sack but it is flour or bread; this silk cloth with weft of mixed silk and wool, even if warp is pure silk; valid if opposite with option if the weft is from something else, then the outer is unspecified; unlike if the lining is unspecified, then it concludes with option. Among what they permitted and excepted from this rule is what is in al-Qunyah: things customarily taken without sale, like lentils, salt, oil, etc., then the vendor buys them back as outgoing; so it is correct, thus sale of non-existent is allowed here. And the sale of what is not valuable property does not conclude, like the sale of a free person, a mudabbar absolutely, umm walad, mukatib, part-free slave, and their children except the child of a mukatib who buys himself in his kitabah; carcass, blood, slaughter of Majusi, apostate, idolator, child who does not understand, insane person, sacrificial animal of a Muhrim whether from halal or haram, slaughter of game from Haram, and game of a Muhrim except sale and his agent; skin of carcass before tanning, pig skin absolutely, its bone, hair, sinew as the correct view, like human hair and bone; about dog bone two narrations. The sale of wine and pig is not valid for the Muslim; as for the dhimmi, it is valid but they differed whether permissible for him or prohibited, and the correct is the second as in al-Bada'i because they consider it property. If they trade then one becomes Muslim before possession, the sale is rescinded. If they exchange a loan then the lender becomes Muslim, he has nothing of the wine; if the borrower becomes Muslim, he must pay its value in one narration, in another like the first. The sale of bees and silkworms is not valid except as a follow-on; nor pure dung, unlike manure mixed with soil; likewise the sale of musical instruments according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) contrary to Abu Hanifa. The sale of unfertilized eggs, fetuses, stud fees, and woman's milk is not valid. In al-Talwih: valuable property is what must be preserved in specie. For the branch and the next branch from al-Qunyah also: the sale of exemptions (barra'at). And the author's statement: it appears that what is in al-Qunyah is weak due to their consensus that sale of non-existent is invalid, likewise unowned property. What prevents what is taken from lentils etc. from being a sale by mutual dealing (ta'ati) and not needing statement of price because it is known? But the share of the imam is not owned before possession, so how can its sale be valid? Keep in mind what Ibn Wahban said in Kitab al-Shurb: what is in al-Qunyah if contrary to the rules is not relied upon unless supported by transmission. Al-Hamawi said: regarding taking lentils etc. as sale by mutual dealing and not needing statement of price, it is disputable because prices vary, leading to disputes. And you are aware that what is in al-Nahr is based on knowledge; then it is said if it is known.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage details the conditions that the object of sale must satisfy: existence (wujud), being valuable property (mal mutaqawwim), being owned (mamluk), being owned by the seller (if selling for himself), and being capable of delivery (maqdur al-taslim). It then provides a long list of things whose sale is invalid because they are non-existent, not property, not owned, or not deliverable. Examples of non-existent things include unborn animals, milk in udder, fruits before they appear, etc. Also objects sold with an attribute that turns out to be false (e.g., ruby turns out to be glass) are invalid unless a condition of option is made. An exception is made for items customarily taken back by vendors like lentils, salt, oil—these are considered sellable even though technically non-existent at the time of purchase because it is a known practice. The passage then lists items that are not valuable property (ghayr mutaqawwim): free humans, certain categories of slaves, carcass, blood, the slaughter of apostates, etc. Wine and pig are not valid for Muslims but for dhimmis there is ikhtilaf; the correct view is that it is prohibited for them as well, but they own it as property. If one party converts to Islam before possession, the sale dissolves. Bees and silkworms are not sold alone but only as part of the hive. Pure dung is not sold, but manure mixed with soil is. Musical instruments are invalid according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, but Abu Hanifa allowed it. Finally, the text discusses a critique of al-Qunyah's exception, noting that many scholars consider it weak because it contradicts the rule that non-existent things cannot be sold, and the prices may be unknown, leading to disputes. Thus the relied-upon position is that such transactions must be valid sales by mutual dealing (ta'ati) only if the price is known.

Key Terms
المعدوم (al-ma'dum): A non-existent item, such as an unborn animal or future fruit, whose sale is invalid.
بيع التعاطي (bay' al-ta'ati): A sale by mutual delivery without verbal offer and acceptance, considered valid for known items.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer purchases a cloth described as 'Herawi' but receives a 'Marwi' cloth. No option was stipulated. Which ruling applies?
A) Sale is valid, buyer must keep the cloth.
B) Sale is sahih but the buyer has the right to return it.
C) Sale is batil due to attribute mismatch.
D) Sale is batil due to non-existence.

Key Takeaway
The object of sale must exist at the time of contract and match its description; otherwise the sale is void unless an option is stipulated for attribute mistakes or a custom exception applies.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Invalid Sales Due to Object Not Being Valuable Property
List of items that are not valuable property, with specific rulings for wine/pig and conversion effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which items are not considered valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) and thus cannot be sold, including the special rulings for wine/pig and conversion.

Core Idea
Only items that are legally valuable property can be sold; free humans, impure substances, and certain carcasses are not salable.

The Hanafi school defines valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) as that which has legal commercial value and is pure or can be purified. This lesson covers the long list of items that fail this condition and the rulings for dhimmis regarding wine and pig.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of mal mutaqawwim
Valuable property is that which is legally owned and has permissible use. Items that are impure (najis) or not owned (free humans) are not mal mutaqawwim.
This condition is separate from existence.
2
Categorize the examples
The passage lists: free humans and their categories (mudabbar, umm walad, etc.), carcasses, blood, certain slaughterings, skins, bones, etc. Understand why each is not valuable.
Some items like dog bone have two narrations.
3
Analyze the wine and pig rulings for Muslims and dhimmis
For Muslims, wine and pig are not mal mutaqawwim, so their sale is invalid. For dhimmis, they are valuable property in their view, so the sale is valid but the correct position is that it is prohibited for them as well.
The ruling changes if one party converts to Islam before possession.
4
Apply to conversion scenarios
If two dhimmis trade wine and one converts before possession, the sale is rescinded. If a loan of wine is given and the lender converts, he gets nothing; if the borrower converts, he must pay the value (in one narration).
These are specific applications of the principle.

As for the conditions of the object of sale: it must be existing, valuable property, owned in itself, owned by the seller if he sells for himself, and capable of delivery. Key Terms
المال المتقوم (al-mal al-mutaqawwim): Property that is legally recognized and has commercial value; excludes impure substances and free humans.
الذمي (al-dhimmi): A non-Muslim citizen of an Islamic state who pays jizyah and is allowed to practice his religion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Which of the following sales is considered valid according to the Hanafi school?

Key Takeaway
Only items that are legally valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) can be sold; free humans, impure items like carcass and wine (for Muslims), and certain slaughtered animals are excluded.

Intra-Hanafi Disputes: Musical Instruments, Bees, and the al-Qunyah Exception
Disputed items within Hanafi school: musical instruments, bees, dung, and the al-Qunyah exception critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the intra-Hanafi disagreements on the sale of musical instruments, bees, and the al-Qunyah exception, and state the mufta bihi (relied-upon) positions.

Core Idea
Musical instruments are prohibited from sale (Sahibayn's view); bees cannot be sold alone; the al-Qunyah exception is weak and not relied upon.

Within the Hanafi school, there are notable differences on certain items. Imam Abu Hanifa allowed the sale of musical instruments, but his two main students (Sahibayn) prohibited it. Similarly, the exception from al-Qunyah allowing sale of non-existent customary items is considered weak. This lesson clarifies these disputes and the final fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed items
The passage explicitly mentions: musical instruments (alat al-malahi), bees and silkworms, pure dung vs mixed, and the al-Qunyah exception.
Also dog bone had two narrations, but that was covered in the previous lesson.
2
Analyze the positions on musical instruments
Imam Abu Hanifa allowed the sale of musical instruments (like drums), but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) prohibited it. The text states 'according to the two, contrary to the Imam'.
The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is the prohibition (Sahibayn's view).
3
Understand the rules for bees and silkworms
Bees and silkworms cannot be sold alone but can be sold as part of the hive or cocoon (taba'an).
This is because they are not considered independent objects of sale.
4
Evaluate the al-Qunyah exception
The exception from al-Qunyah allowed the sale of non-existent items like lentils taken back by custom. However, later scholars (like Ibn Wahban and al-Hamawi) criticized it as weak because it contradicts consensus and leads to price uncertainty.
The correct view is that such transactions are valid only as bay' al-ta'ati (mutual delivery) if the price is known.

As for the conditions of the object of sale: it must be existing, valuable property, owned in itself, owned by the seller if he sells for himself, and capable of delivery. Non-existence includes: the offspring of offspring, the fetus, milk in the udder, fruit and crops before appearance, seeds in watermelon, pits in dates, meat on a live sheep, fat, tail, legs, head; sesame oil in sesame; this gem supposed to be ruby but it is glass; this Herawi cloth but it is Marwi; this slave but it is a female slave; a house thought to be brick but it is adobe; a cloth thought dyed with safflower but it is saffron; wheat in a sack but it is flour or bread; this silk cloth with weft of mixed silk and wool, even if warp is pure silk; valid if opposite with option if the weft is from something else, then the outer is unspecified; unlike if the lining is unspecified, then it concludes with option. Among what they permitted and excepted from this rule is what is in al-Qunyah: things customarily taken without sale, like lentils, salt, oil, etc., then the vendor buys them back as outgoing; so it is correct, thus sale of non-existent is allowed here. And the sale of what is not valuable property does not conclude, like the sale of a free person, a mudabbar absolutely, umm walad, mukatib, part-free slave, and their children except the child of a mukatib who buys himself in his kitabah; carcass, blood, slaughter of Majusi, apostate, idolator, child who does not understand, insane person, sacrificial animal of a Muhrim whether from halal or haram, slaughter of game from Haram, and game of a Muhrim except sale and his agent; skin of carcass before tanning, pig skin absolutely, its bone, hair, sinew as the correct view, like human hair and bone; about dog bone two narrations. The sale of wine and pig is not valid for the Muslim; as for the dhimmi, it is valid but they differed whether permissible for him or prohibited, and the correct is the second as in al-Bada'i because they consider it property. If they trade then one becomes Muslim before possession, the sale is rescinded. If they exchange a loan then the lender becomes Muslim, he has nothing of the wine; if the borrower becomes Muslim, he must pay its value in one narration, in another like the first. The sale of bees and silkworms is not valid except as a follow-on; nor pure dung, unlike manure mixed with soil; likewise the sale of musical instruments according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) contrary to Abu Hanifa. The sale of unfertilized eggs, fetuses, stud fees, and woman's milk is not valid. In al-Talwih: valuable property is what must be preserved in specie. For the branch and the next branch from al-Qunyah also: the sale of exemptions (barra'at). And the author's statement: it appears that what is in al-Qunyah is weak due to their consensus that sale of non-existent is invalid, likewise unowned property. What prevents what is taken from lentils etc. from being a sale by mutual dealing (ta'ati) and not needing statement of price because it is known? But the share of the imam is not owned before possession, so how can its sale be valid? Keep in mind what Ibn Wahban said in Kitab al-Shurb: what is in al-Qunyah if contrary to the rules is not relied upon unless supported by transmission. Al-Hamawi said: regarding taking lentils etc. as sale by mutual dealing and not needing statement of price, it is disputable because prices vary, leading to disputes. And you are aware that what is in al-Nahr is based on knowledge; then it is said if it is known.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage details the conditions that the object of sale must satisfy: existence (wujud), being valuable property (mal mutaqawwim), being owned (mamluk), being owned by the seller (if selling for himself), and being capable of delivery (maqdur al-taslim). It then provides a long list of things whose sale is invalid because they are non-existent, not property, not owned, or not deliverable. Examples of non-existent things include unborn animals, milk in udder, fruits before they appear, etc. Also objects sold with an attribute that turns out to be false (e.g., ruby turns out to be glass) are invalid unless a condition of option is made. An exception is made for items customarily taken back by vendors like lentils, salt, oil—these are considered sellable even though technically non-existent at the time of purchase because it is a known practice. The passage then lists items that are not valuable property (ghayr mutaqawwim): free humans, certain categories of slaves, carcass, blood, the slaughter of apostates, etc. Wine and pig are not valid for Muslims but for dhimmis there is ikhtilaf; the correct view is that it is prohibited for them as well, but they own it as property. If one party converts to Islam before possession, the sale dissolves. Bees and silkworms are not sold alone but only as part of the hive. Pure dung is not sold, but manure mixed with soil is. Musical instruments are invalid according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, but Abu Hanifa allowed it. Finally, the text discusses a critique of al-Qunyah's exception, noting that many scholars consider it weak because it contradicts the rule that non-existent things cannot be sold, and the prices may be unknown, leading to disputes. Thus the relied-upon position is that such transactions must be valid sales by mutual dealing (ta'ati) only if the price is known.

Key Terms
آلات الملاهي (alat al-malahi): Musical instruments that are used for entertainment and often lead to prohibited activities.
بيع التعاطي (bay' al-ta'ati): A sale by mutual delivery without verbal offer and acceptance, considered valid for known items.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is the mufta bihi ruling on the sale of musical instruments in the Hanafi school?
A) Valid, as per Abu Hanifa.
B) Invalid, as per Sahibayn.
C) Valid only for private use.
D) Invalid only if played publicly.

Key Takeaway
Intra-Hanafi disputes require careful attention; the mufta bihi for musical instruments is prohibition, and the al-Qunyah exception is weak and not operative.

Invalid Sales: Non-Existent and Misdescribed Items
Listing examples of invalid sales due to non-existence or misdescription

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which items are considered non-existent (ma'dum) in a sale, state the ruling of batil, and distinguish between void sales and valid sales with khiyar for misdescription.

Core Idea
A sale of a non-existent item is void; misdescription gives the buyer an option to cancel.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq illustrates the Hanafi rule that the subject matter of a sale must exist at the time of contract. It lists numerous examples of items deemed non-existent (ma‘dūm), such as unborn animals and milk in the udder, leading to void sales. It also covers misdescribed items, where the sale is valid but the buyer has the right to cancel. Finally, a concession from al-Qunyah allows sale of certain customarily taken items despite apparent non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Recognize that the passage covers two distinct grounds for invalidating a sale: non-existence of the subject matter (ma‘dūm) and misdescription (different from what was specified).
Non-existence makes the sale batil; misdescription makes it sahih but with khiyar.
2
Analyze non-existence examples
List the examples given: unborn animals, milk in udder, unsprouted crops, meat on a live animal, etc. Understand that these items do not yet exist as separate property.
The sale is void because the subject matter is not mawjud at the time of contract.
3
Analyze misdescription examples
Note examples like a gemstone said to be ruby but found to be glass, or cloth described as Herawi but found to be Marwi. In such cases, the sale is valid but the buyer has khiyar (option to cancel).
The defect is in the description, not in existence.
4
Examine the concession from al-Qunyah
Read the exception: items taken by custom without a sale (e.g., lentils, salt, oil) can be validly sold even if the exact specification is not made, as a dispensation.
This is a limited exception, considered weak by later authorities.
5
Apply to new scenarios
Given a hypothetical sale, determine whether the issue is non-existence (void) or misdescription (valid with khiyar), and state the ruling accordingly.
Focus on the condition of existence versus description accuracy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العدم كنتاج النتاج والحمل واللبن في الضرع والثمر والزرع قبل الظهور، والبزر في البطيخ، والنوى في التمر، واللحم في الشاة الحية والشحم والألية فيها وأكارعها ورأسها، والسجير في السمسم، وهذا الفص على أنه ياقوت فإذا هو زجاج، أو هذا الثوب الهروي فإذا هو مروي، أو هذا العبد فإذا هو جارية، أو دار على أن بناءها آجر فإذا هو لبن، أو ثوب على أنه مصبوغ بعصفر فإذا هو بزعفران، أو هو حنطة في جوالق فإذا هي دقيق، أو خبز، أو هذا الثوب القز فإذا لحمته من ملحم ولو كان سداه من قز، وصح لو دقيق فإذا هي كان عكسه مع الخيار إذا اللحمة . من كذا فإذا الظهارة من غير المعين بخلاف ما إذا كانت البطانة من غير المعين فإنه ينعقد مع الخيار. ومما تسامحوا فيه وأخرجوه عن هذه القاعدة ما في القنية : الأشياء التي تؤخذ من العادة من غير بيع كالعدس والملح والزيت ونحوها ثم اشتراها البياع على وجه الخرج كما هو صح اهـ. فيجوز بيع المعدوم هنا.
Non-existence like the product of a product, the fetus, milk in the udder, fruit and crops before appearance, seeds in a watermelon, pits in dates, meat on a live sheep, fat, tail, trotters, and head, sesame oil in sesame seeds; and this gemstone described as ruby but it turns out to be glass, or this cloth described as Herawi but it turns out to be Marwi, or this slave described as male but it turns out to be female, or a house described as brick-built but it turns out to be mud-brick, or cloth described as dyed with safflower but it turns out to be saffron, or wheat in a sack but it turns out to be flour or bread, or this silk cloth whose weft is from mixed silk even if warp is pure silk, and it is valid if the opposite with option when the weft is from something unspecified [؟] as opposed to when the lining is from unspecified material, then it is concluded with option. And among things they permitted and made an exception to this rule is what is in al-Qunyah: items taken by custom without a sale like lentils, salt, oil, and similar items, then the seller buys them on the face of taking out as is, so it is valid. So here sale of non-existent is permitted.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is a commentary on the conditions of the subject matter in a sale. It begins by listing items that are considered non-existent at the time of contract, such as unborn animals, unsprouted crops, and certain parts of a living animal. The sale of such items is invalid because the subject matter must be mawjud (existing) at the time of the contract. The author then gives examples of misdescription, where the item is described as one thing but turns out to be another, e.g., a gemstone thought to be ruby but actually glass. In such cases, the buyer has the option (khiyar) to cancel the sale. However, if the added or lining material is unspecified, the sale is concluded with khiyar. Finally, a concession from al-Qunyah is mentioned: certain commodities customarily taken without a sale (like lentils, salt, oil) can be validly sold even if the exact quantity or quality is not specified, as a dispensation from the general rule.

Key Terms
معدوم (ma'dum): Something that does not exist at the time of the contract, making the sale invalid.
خيار (khiyar): The option to cancel a sale due to a defect or misdescription.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of misdescription (different from specified) leading to a valid sale with khiyar?
A) Selling milk in the udder.
B) Selling a gemstone described as ruby but found to be glass.
C) Selling a house before it is built.
D) Selling a live sheep with its meat.

Key Takeaway
Sale of a non-existent item is void; sale of a misdescribed item is valid but gives the buyer the option to cancel.

Mal Mutaqawwim: The Foundation of Valid Sales
Listing non-mutaqawwim items and their rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define mal mutaqawwim and identify items that fail this condition, resulting in a batil sale.

Core Idea
Only mal mutaqawwim can be sold; free persons, impure substances, and certain animals are not property.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq establishes a core principle: the subject matter of a sale must be mal mutaqawwim—legally recognized property that can be benefitted from. The author lists numerous items that are not considered mutaqawwim in Hanafi law, such as free humans, carrion, blood, and pigs. Understanding this list is essential to avoid invalid contracts.

Guided Path
1
Define mal mutaqawwim
Use the definition from al-Talwih: 'what must be preserved in kind.' Identify that it must be lawful in benefit and have monetary value in Muslim society.
Non-Muslims may own wine as wealth, but it is not mutaqawwim for Muslims.
2
Identify the categories of non-mutaqawwim items
Group the list: persons (free, mudabbar, etc.), impure items (carrion, blood, pig parts), certain slaughtered animals, and animals not considered property (bees, silkworms).
The sale of human hair and bones is also invalid on the sound opinion.
3
Apply the ruling to a hypothetical
Given a scenario (e.g., selling a dog's bone or musical instrument), state the hukm and note any intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
For musical instruments, Abu Hanifa allowed the sale, but the majority (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) prohibit it.

والمجنون ومذبوح صيد المحرم، سواء كان من الحل أو الحرم ومذبوح صيد الحرم وصيد المحرم إلا بيع و وكيله ، وجلد الميتة قبل الدبغ وجلد الخنزير مطلقاً وعظمه وشعره وعصبه على الصحيح كشعر الآدمي وعظمه، وفي عظم أو فعلاً، وكذا الطير في الهواء بعد أن كان في يده وطار والسمك بعد الصيد والإلقاء في الحظيرة إذا كان لا يمكن أخذه إلا بصيد ولا ينعقد بيع الدين من غير من عليه الدين، ويجوز من المديون لعدم الحاجة إلى التسليم ولم ينعقد بيع المغصوب من غير الغاصب إذا كان الغاصب منكراً له ولا بينة. وإلى هنا صارت شرائط الانعقاد أحد عشر ؛ اثنان في العاقد، واثنان في العقد، وواحد في مكانه، وستة في المعقود عليه.
وأما شرائط النفاذ فالملك أو الولاية فلم ينعقد بيع الفضولي عندنا، وأما شراؤه فنافذ كما سيأتي، والولاية إما بإنابة المالك أو الشارع. فالأول الوكالة والثاني ولاية الأب ومن قام
،
يكون بيعاً بالتعاطي وانظر ما يأتي عن الولوالجية في شرح قوله ولا بد من معرف رفة قدر ووصف ثمن. قوله : (أحد عشر ) صوابه تسعة قوله : (فلم ينعقد بيع الفضولي عندنا) صوابه فلم ينفذ إلا أن يريد بيع الفضولي لنفسه فإنه باطل لكن قد علمت مما قدمناه عن الرملي أنه على الرواية الضعيفة
And the sale of what is not valuable property is not concluded, such as the sale of a free person, a mudabbar absolutely, an umm walad, a mukatab, a partial freedman, and their children, except the child of a mukatab purchased in his kitaba; carrion, blood, the slaughtered of a Magian, an apostate, an idolater, a child who does not understand, and an insane person; the slaughtered of a muhrim’s hunt, whether from halal or haram, and the slaughtered of the Haram hunt and the muhrim’s hunt, except the sale of his agent; the skin of carrion before tanning, and the skin of a pig absolutely, its bone, hair, and sinew according to the sound opinion, like human hair and bone; and regarding a dog’s bone there are two narrations. And the sale of wine and pig is not concluded for a Muslim, but for a dhimmi it is concluded, though they differed whether it is permissible for him or prohibited; the correct is the second as in al-Bada’i’ because they consider it wealth. If they transact and then one becomes Muslim before taking possession, the sale is dissolved. If they exchange a loan and then the lender becomes Muslim, he has nothing from the wine; if the borrower becomes Muslim, he owes its value in one narration, and in another like the first. And the sale of bees and silkworms is not concluded except as ancillary; nor the sale of pure human waste contrary to animal dung and that mixed with soil; likewise the sale of musical instruments according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) contrary to Imam Abu Hanifa. And the sale of mulaeqih, madamin, stud fees, and women’s milk is not concluded. In al-Talwih: mutaqawwim is what must be preserved in kind. For the branch and the upcoming branch from al-Qunyah, which is the sale of patents (bar’aat) and mention of the author’s words, I say: apparently what is in al-Qunyah is weak due to their agreement that sale of non-existent is invalid, as is non-owned. What prevents the taken from lentils etc. from being a sale by mu’atah, not needing specification of price because it is known? And the imam’s share is not owned before receipt, so how can its sale be valid? Be mindful of what Ibn Wahban said in Kitab al-Shurb: if al-Qunyah contradicts principles, it is ignored unless supported by transmission. Al-Hamawi said: considering the taken from lentils etc. as sale by mu’atah not needing specification of price is problematic because their prices vary, leading to dispute. And you know that what is in al-Nahr is based on knowledge of it; so it is said if it is known by its like or its value. And wine must be avoided by text, so it is not valuable. In al-Qunyah: the minimum value required for validity of sale is a fals, even if it is a piece of bread; the purchase of patents written by the diwan on workers is invalid. It was said to him: the imams of Bukhara permitted the sale of shares of imams. He said: because waqf property exists there, not here. So on this basis, for a beneficiary in schools, selling his bread before receipt from the supervisor is permissible, unlike a soldier if he sells the barley specified for his animal’s fodder before receipt. And the sale of grass even in his owned land, and water in his river or in what he does not own, is not concluded; the sale of game, firewood, and grass before acquisition; and the sale of Makkan land according to Imam; and land revived without the imam’s permission according to Imam; and market shops whose income goes to the sultan due to lack of ownership, because the sultan only permitted them to build but did not grant the land, as in al-Bada’i’. In al-Qunyah: if one digs his well and sells a place from the mine, then sells that excavation or rents it, it is invalid because he only owns what is taken out; what remains stays permissible. May Allah be pleased with him: this is a narration in an incident that reached me about some reckless mufti who gave a fatwa that if someone digs stone in a mountain for making pots and then dies and another carves pots from it, the heir of the digger can prevent it; but the correct is that they cannot prevent, because the remaining stone, though appearing due to his digging, remains on original permissibility. And we exclude by our saying: it must be owned by the seller; so the sale of what is not owned by him is not concluded, even if he later comes to own it, except salam and the usurped item: if the usurper sells it and then guarantees its value, his sale is effective because the ownership is traced back to the time of sale, so it becomes clear that he sold his own property. And we said concerning what he sells for himself to exclude the agent and the faḍūlī: the first is effective, the second is concluded and suspended. And we said: it must be capable of delivery; so the sale of something the seller cannot deliver is not concluded, like the sale of an absconding slave in the apparent narration: if he appears, the contract needs renewal by word or action. Similarly, a bird in the air after it was in his hand and flew, and fish after capture and release into a pond if it cannot be caught except by fishing. And the sale of a debt from someone other than the debtor is not concluded, but from the debtor is valid because no need for delivery. And the sale of a usurped item from someone other than the usurper is not concluded if the usurper denies it and there is no evidence. Up to here, the conditions for conclusion (shurūṭ al-in‘iqād) became eleven: two in the contracting parties, two in the contract, one in its place, and six in the subject matter. As for the conditions for execution (shurūṭ al-nafādh): ownership or guardianship; so the sale of a faḍūlī is not effective according to us, but his purchase is effective as will come. Guardianship is either by appointment of the owner or by the Lawgiver. The first is agency, the second is guardianship of the father and whoever stands [in his place]. It is a sale by mu‘āṭāh; and see what comes from al-Walwālījī in explanation of his statement: ‘It is necessary to know the quantity and description of the price.’ His statement: ‘eleven’ – the correct is nine. His statement: ‘so the sale of the faḍūlī is not effective’ – the correct is ‘not executed’, unless he means the faḍūlī selling for himself, then it is invalid, but you have learned from what we presented from al-Ramlī that it is on the weak narration.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This extensive passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq continues the discussion of invalid sales due to defects in the subject matter. It categorically states that only mal mutaqawwim (valuable property) can be sold. It then lists items that are not mal mutaqawwim: free persons, mudabbars, umm walad, mukatabs, etc.; carrion, blood, and certain animals slaughtered by non-believers; parts of pigs and humans; wine and pigs (with a distinction between Muslims and dhimmis); bees, silkworms, human waste (except when mixed with soil), musical instruments (according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and certain uncertain things like mulaeqih (what is in the womb) and madamin (what is in the back). The passage then engages in a critical discussion of a concession from al-Qunyah regarding the sale of patents (bar'aat) and the custom of taking items like lentils, concluding that such concessions are weak. It also addresses ownership and delivery: the seller must own the item, and it must be capable of delivery. Exceptions include salam (advance sale) and usurped goods when the usurper guarantees value. The passage concludes by summarizing the conditions for the validity of a sale contract: eleven conditions (later corrected to nine) related to parties, contract, place, and subject matter, and conditions for executory power (ownership or guardianship).

Key Terms
مال متقوم (mal mutaqawwim): Property that is legally recognized and has a monetary value; items that must be preserved in kind according to the Law.
فضولي (fuduli): A person who sells property without being the owner or having any authorization; such a sale is suspended until ratified.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on the sale of a pig's skin before tanning?
A) The sale is valid because it has potential use.
B) The sale is batil because it is not mal mutaqawwim.
C) The sale is valid only if sold to a dhimmi.
D) The sale is makruh tanzihi.

Key Takeaway
A valid sale requires mal mutaqawwim; items like free persons, impure substances, and certain animals cannot be sold.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Ownership and Delivery: Essential Conditions for Sale
Conditions of ownership and delivery for sale validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a sale is invalid due to lack of ownership or delivery capability, and distinguish the rulings for fuduli, salam, and usurped goods.

Core Idea
The seller must own the item and be able to deliver it; otherwise, the sale is batil unless an exception applies.

After establishing that the subject must be mal mutaqawwim, the author turns to two more prerequisites: ownership and deliverability. A person cannot sell what he does not own, nor what he cannot hand over. However, there are exceptions: salam (advance sale) and the case of a usurper who sells then guarantees the item. This lesson unpacks these rules and their nuances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ownership requirement
The seller must own the item at the time of contract. If he sells what he does not own, the sale is not concluded (batil). Exceptions: salam (future delivery) and usurper's sale if he later guarantees value.
The phrase 'فلم ينعقد بيع ما ليس بمملوك له' is clear.
2
Examine the delivery condition
The item must be capable of delivery at the time of sale. Examples of failure: an absconding slave (abaq), a bird that flew away, fish in a pond that must be caught again. If the item becomes deliverable later, the contract needs renewal.
Kapalability is assessed at the time of contract.
3
Apply to the fuduli case
A fuduli (unauthorized seller) sells a non-owned item. The sale is mawquf (suspended) pending ratification. This differs from the batil sale of an item he does not own at all.
The fuduli lacks ownership; his sale is not effective without ratification.

And the sale of what is not valuable property is not concluded, such as the sale of a free person, a mudabbar absolutely, an umm walad, a mukatab, a partial freedman, and their children, except the child of a mukatab purchased in his kitaba; carrion, blood, the slaughtered of a Magian, an apostate, an idolater, a child who does not understand, and an insane person; the slaughtered of a muhrim’s hunt, whether from halal or haram, and the slaughtered of the Haram hunt and the muhrim’s hunt, except the sale of his agent; the skin of carrion before tanning, and the skin of a pig absolutely, its bone, hair, and sinew according to the sound opinion, like human hair and bone; and regarding a dog’s bone there are two narrations. And the sale of wine and pig is not concluded for a Muslim, but for a dhimmi it is concluded, though they differed whether it is permissible for him or prohibited; the correct is the second as in al-Bada’i’ because they consider it wealth. If they transact and then one becomes Muslim before taking possession, the sale is dissolved. If they exchange a loan and then the lender becomes Muslim, he has nothing from the wine; if the borrower becomes Muslim, he owes its value in one narration, and in another like the first. And the sale of bees and silkworms is not concluded except as ancillary; nor the sale of pure human waste contrary to animal dung and that mixed with soil; likewise the sale of musical instruments according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) contrary to Imam Abu Hanifa. And the sale of mulaeqih, madamin, stud fees, and women’s milk is not concluded. In al-Talwih: mutaqawwim is what must be preserved in kind. For the branch and the upcoming branch from al-Qunyah, which is the sale of patents (bar’aat) and mention of the author’s words, I say: apparently what is in al-Qunyah is weak due to their agreement that sale of non-existent is invalid, as is non-owned. What prevents the taken from lentils etc. from being a sale by mu’atah, not needing specification of price because it is known? And the imam’s share is not owned before receipt, so how can its sale be valid? Be mindful of what Ibn Wahban said in Kitab al-Shurb: if al-Qunyah contradicts principles, it is ignored unless supported by transmission. Al-Hamawi said: considering the taken from lentils etc. as sale by mu’atah not needing specification of price is problematic because their prices vary, leading to dispute. And you know that what is in al-Nahr is based on knowledge of it; so it is said if it is known by its like or its value. And wine must be avoided by text, so it is not valuable. In al-Qunyah: the minimum value required for validity of sale is a fals, even if it is a piece of bread; the purchase of patents written by the diwan on workers is invalid. It was said to him: the imams of Bukhara permitted the sale of shares of imams. He said: because waqf property exists there, not here. So on this basis, for a beneficiary in schools, selling his bread before receipt from the supervisor is permissible, unlike a soldier if he sells the barley specified for his animal’s fodder before receipt. And the sale of grass even in his owned land, and water in his river or in what he does not own, is not concluded; the sale of game, firewood, and grass before acquisition; and the sale of Makkan land according to Imam; and land revived without the imam’s permission according to Imam; and market shops whose income goes to the sultan due to lack of ownership, because the sultan only permitted them to build but did not grant the land, as in al-Bada’i’. In al-Qunyah: if one digs his well and sells a place from the mine, then sells that excavation or rents it, it is invalid because he only owns what is taken out; what remains stays permissible. May Allah be pleased with him: this is a narration in an incident that reached me about some reckless mufti who gave a fatwa that if someone digs stone in a mountain for making pots and then dies and another carves pots from it, the heir of the digger can prevent it; but the correct is that they cannot prevent, because the remaining stone, though appearing due to his digging, remains on original permissibility. And we exclude by our saying: it must be owned by the seller; so the sale of what is not owned by him is not concluded, even if he later comes to own it, except salam and the usurped item: if the usurper sells it and then guarantees its value, his sale is effective because the ownership is traced back to the time of sale, so it becomes clear that he sold his own property. And we said concerning what he sells for himself to exclude the agent and the faḍūlī: the first is effective, the second is concluded and suspended. And we said: it must be capable of delivery; so the sale of something the seller cannot deliver is not concluded, like the sale of an absconding slave in the apparent narration: if he appears, the contract needs renewal by word or action. Similarly, a bird in the air after it was in his hand and flew, and fish after capture and release into a pond if it cannot be caught except by fishing. And the sale of a debt from someone other than the debtor is not concluded, but from the debtor is valid because no need for delivery. And the sale of a usurped item from someone other than the usurper is not concluded if the usurper denies it and there is no evidence. Up to here, the conditions for conclusion (shurūṭ al-in‘iqād) became eleven: two in the contracting parties, two in the contract, one in its place, and six in the subject matter. As for the conditions for execution (shurūṭ al-nafādh): ownership or guardianship; so the sale of a faḍūlī is not effective according to us, but his purchase is effective as will come. Guardianship is either by appointment of the owner or by the Lawgiver. The first is agency, the second is guardianship of the father and whoever stands [in his place]. It is a sale by mu‘āṭāh; and see what comes from al-Walwālījī in explanation of his statement: ‘It is necessary to know the quantity and description of the price.’ His statement: ‘eleven’ – the correct is nine. His statement: ‘so the sale of the faḍūlī is not effective’ – the correct is ‘not executed’, unless he means the faḍūlī selling for himself, then it is invalid, but you have learned from what we presented from al-Ramlī that it is on the weak narration.

Special Cases and the Summary of Sales Conditions
Edge cases and summary of conditions for sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conditions for a valid sale to special cases (bees, musical instruments, patents) and recall the total number of conditions.

Core Idea
Certain items like bees, musical instruments, and patents require careful analysis; exceptions may exist by custom but are weak.

The final part of the passage addresses edge cases: the sale of bees, silkworms, human waste (mixed with soil), musical instruments, and patents ('bar'aat'). It also critically examines a concession from al-Qunyah that allowed certain sales by custom, concluding that such concessions are weak. The passage ends by summarizing the conditions for a valid sale: eleven (corrected to nine) conditions for conclusion, plus two for execution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the special cases that are invalid
Bees and silkworms can only be sold as part of a larger transaction (ancillary). Musical instruments: invalid according to the majority. Patents: the author criticizes a weak ruling from al-Qunyah that allowed their sale.
The discussion on patents is a critical refinement.
2
Analyze the critical discussion of al-Qunyah
The author rejects al-Qunyah's allowance for selling patents by custom, citing consensus (ijma') that sale of non-existent items is invalid. He supports this with Ibn Wahban's statement that such concessions are ignored unless supported by transmission.
This shows the importance of principles over local customs.
3
Recall the conditions for a valid sale
The passage ends with a count: eleven conditions (later corrected to nine) for the conclusion (in'iqad) of the sale, plus conditions for execution (nafadh): ownership or guardianship. The nine include: two in the contracting parties (sanity, maturity), two in the contract (offer and acceptance), one in its place (the majlis), and six in the subject matter (being property, owned, deliverable, etc.).
These conditions are foundational in Hanafi fiqh.

أيضاً وهو بيع البراءات وذكره لكلام المؤلف أقول: الظاهر أن ما في القنية ضعيف لاتفاق كلمتهم على أن بيع المعدوم لا يصح وكذا غير المملوك، وما المانع من أن يكون المأخوذ من العدس ونحوه بيعاً بالتعاطي ولا يحتاج في مثله إلى بيان الثمن لأنه معلوم كما سيأتي وحظ الإمام لا يملك قبل القبض فأني يصح بيعه؟ وكن على ذكر مما قاله ابن وهبان في كتاب الشرب ما في القنية إذا كان مخالفاً للقواعد لا التفات إليه ما لم يعضه نقل .اهـ. قال الحموي في كون المأخوذ من العدس ونحوه بيعاً بالتعاطي وأنه لا يحتاج في مثله إلى بيان الثمن نظر، لأن أثمان هذه تختلف فيفضي إلى المنازعة .اهـ. وأنت خبير بأن ما في النهر مبني على العلم به فحينئذ يقال إن كان معلوماً أو بمثله أو بقيمته، والخمر يجب اجتنابها بالنص فلم تكن متقومة اهـ. وفي القنية: أدنى القيمة التي تشترط لجواز البيع فلس ولو كانت كسرة خبز لا يجوز شراء البراءات التي يكتبها الديوان على العمال لا يصح . قيل له : أئمة بخارى جوزوا بيع حظوظ الأئمة . قال : لأن مال الوقف قائم ثمة ولا كذلك هنا ا هـ . فعلى هذا يجوز للمستحق في المدارس بيع خبزه قبل قبضه من المشرف بخلاف الجندي إذا باع الشعير المعين لعلف دابته قبل قبضه. وخرج الكلأ ولو في أرض مملوكة له، والماء في نهره أو في ما لا يملكه فلم ينعقد بيع بيع الصيد والحطب والحشيش قبل الإحراز وبيع أرض مكة عند الإمام، وأرض أحياها بغير إذن الإمام عند الإمام، وحوانيت السوق التي عليها غلة للسلطان لعدم الملك لأن السلطان إنما أذن لهم في البناء ولم يجعل البقعة لهم كما في البدائع. وفي القنية: حفر بئره، وبيع موضعاً من المعدن ثم باع تلك الحفيرة أو أجرها لا يصح لأنه إنما ملك من المعدن ما يخرج ويؤخذ وما بقي فيه بقي على الإباحة قال رضي الله تعالى عنه : وهذه رواية في واقعة بلغتني عن بعض المفتين المجازفين أنه أفتى فيمن حفر في جبل حجراً يتخذ منه القدور ثم مات ونحت غيره منه قدوراً بأن لورثة الحافر المنع تاب الله عليه وعلينا وهداه وإيانا، والصواب ليس لهم المنع لأن الحجر الباقي وإن ظهر بحفره بقي على أصل الإباحة اهـ. وخرج بقولنا وأن يكون ملكاً للبائع ما ليس كذلك فلم ينعقد بيع ما ليس بمملوك له وإن ملكه بعده إلا السلم والمغصوب لو باعه الغاصب ثم ضمن الغاصب قيمته نفذ بيعه لاستناد الملك إلى وقت البيع فتبين أنه باع ملك نفسه. وقلنا فيما يبيعه لنفسه ليخرج النائب والفضولي فالأول نافذ والثاني منعقد موقوفاً وقلنا وأن يكون مقدور التسليم فلم ينعقد بيع معجوز التسليم عند البائع كبيع الآبق في ظاهر الرواية، فإن حضر احتيج إلى تجديد الركن قولاً أو فعلاً، وكذا الطير في الهواء بعد أن كان في يده وطار والسمك بعد الصيد والإلقاء في الحظيرة إذا كان لا يمكن أخذه إلا بصيد ولا ينعقد بيع الدين من غير من عليه الدين، ويجوز من المديون لعدم الحاجة إلى التسليم ولم ينعقد بيع المغصوب من غير الغاصب إذا كان الغاصب منكراً له ولا بينة. وإلى هنا صارت شرائط الانعقاد أحد عشر ؛ اثنان في العاقد، واثنان في العقد، وواحد في مكانه، وستة في المعقود عليه.
وأما شرائط النفاذ فالملك أو الولاية فلم ينعقد بيع الفضولي عندنا، وأما شراؤه فنافذ كما سيأتي، والولاية إما بإنابة المالك أو الشارع. فالأول الوكالة والثاني ولاية الأب ومن قام
،
يكون بيعاً بالتعاطي وانظر ما يأتي عن الولوالجية في شرح قوله ولا بد من معرف رفة قدر ووصف ثمن. قوله : (أحد عشر ) صوابه تسعة قوله : (فلم ينعقد بيع الفضولي عندنا) صوابه فلم ينفذ إلا أن يريد بيع الفضولي لنفسه فإنه باطل لكن قد علمت مما قدمناه عن الرملي أنه على الرواية الضعيفة
And the sale of what is not valuable property is not concluded, such as the sale of a free person, a mudabbar absolutely, an umm walad, a mukatab, a partial freedman, and their children, except the child of a mukatab purchased in his kitaba; carrion, blood, the slaughtered of a Magian, an apostate, an idolater, a child who does not understand, and an insane person; the slaughtered of a muhrim’s hunt, whether from halal or haram, and the slaughtered of the Haram hunt and the muhrim’s hunt, except the sale of his agent; the skin of carrion before tanning, and the skin of a pig absolutely, its bone, hair, and sinew according to the sound opinion, like human hair and bone; and regarding a dog’s bone there are two narrations. And the sale of wine and pig is not concluded for a Muslim, but for a dhimmi it is concluded, though they differed whether it is permissible for him or prohibited; the correct is the second as in al-Bada’i’ because they consider it wealth. If they transact and then one becomes Muslim before taking possession, the sale is dissolved. If they exchange a loan and then the lender becomes Muslim, he has nothing from the wine; if the borrower becomes Muslim, he owes its value in one narration, and in another like the first. And the sale of bees and silkworms is not concluded except as ancillary; nor the sale of pure human waste contrary to animal dung and that mixed with soil; likewise the sale of musical instruments according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) contrary to Imam Abu Hanifa. And the sale of mulaeqih, madamin, stud fees, and women’s milk is not concluded. In al-Talwih: mutaqawwim is what must be preserved in kind. For the branch and the upcoming branch from al-Qunyah, which is the sale of patents (bar’aat) and mention of the author’s words, I say: apparently what is in al-Qunyah is weak due to their agreement that sale of non-existent is invalid, as is non-owned. What prevents the taken from lentils etc. from being a sale by mu’atah, not needing specification of price because it is known? And the imam’s share is not owned before receipt, so how can its sale be valid? Be mindful of what Ibn Wahban said in Kitab al-Shurb: if al-Qunyah contradicts principles, it is ignored unless supported by transmission. Al-Hamawi said: considering the taken from lentils etc. as sale by mu’atah not needing specification of price is problematic because their prices vary, leading to dispute. And you know that what is in al-Nahr is based on knowledge of it; so it is said if it is known by its like or its value. And wine must be avoided by text, so it is not valuable. In al-Qunyah: the minimum value required for validity of sale is a fals, even if it is a piece of bread; the purchase of patents written by the diwan on workers is invalid. It was said to him: the imams of Bukhara permitted the sale of shares of imams. He said: because waqf property exists there, not here. So on this basis, for a beneficiary in schools, selling his bread before receipt from the supervisor is permissible, unlike a soldier if he sells the barley specified for his animal’s fodder before receipt. And the sale of grass even in his owned land, and water in his river or in what he does not own, is not concluded; the sale of game, firewood, and grass before acquisition; and the sale of Makkan land according to Imam; and land revived without the imam’s permission according to Imam; and market shops whose income goes to the sultan due to lack of ownership, because the sultan only permitted them to build but did not grant the land, as in al-Bada’i’. In al-Qunyah: if one digs his well and sells a place from the mine, then sells that excavation or rents it, it is invalid because he only owns what is taken out; what remains stays permissible. May Allah be pleased with him: this is a narration in an incident that reached me about some reckless mufti who gave a fatwa that if someone digs stone in a mountain for making pots and then dies and another carves pots from it, the heir of the digger can prevent it; but the correct is that they cannot prevent, because the remaining stone, though appearing due to his digging, remains on original permissibility. And we exclude by our saying: it must be owned by the seller; so the sale of what is not owned by him is not concluded, even if he later comes to own it, except salam and the usurped item: if the usurper sells it and then guarantees its value, his sale is effective because the ownership is traced back to the time of sale, so it becomes clear that he sold his own property. And we said concerning what he sells for himself to exclude the agent and the faḍūlī: the first is effective, the second is concluded and suspended. And we said: it must be capable of delivery; so the sale of something the seller cannot deliver is not concluded, like the sale of an absconding slave in the apparent narration: if he appears, the contract needs renewal by word or action. Similarly, a bird in the air after it was in his hand and flew, and fish after capture and release into a pond if it cannot be caught except by fishing. And the sale of a debt from someone other than the debtor is not concluded, but from the debtor is valid because no need for delivery. And the sale of a usurped item from someone other than the usurper is not concluded if the usurper denies it and there is no evidence. Up to here, the conditions for conclusion (shurūṭ al-in‘iqād) became eleven: two in the contracting parties, two in the contract, one in its place, and six in the subject matter. As for the conditions for execution (shurūṭ al-nafādh): ownership or guardianship; so the sale of a faḍūlī is not effective according to us, but his purchase is effective as will come. Guardianship is either by appointment of the owner or by the Lawgiver. The first is agency, the second is guardianship of the father and whoever stands [in his place]. It is a sale by mu‘āṭāh; and see what comes from al-Walwālījī in explanation of his statement: ‘It is necessary to know the quantity and description of the price.’ His statement: ‘eleven’ – the correct is nine. His statement: ‘so the sale of the faḍūlī is not effective’ – the correct is ‘not executed’, unless he means the faḍūlī selling for himself, then it is invalid, but you have learned from what we presented from al-Ramlī that it is on the weak narration.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This extensive passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq continues the discussion of invalid sales due to defects in the subject matter. It categorically states that only mal mutaqawwim (valuable property) can be sold. It then lists items that are not mal mutaqawwim: free persons, mudabbars, umm walad, mukatabs, etc.; carrion, blood, and certain animals slaughtered by non-believers; parts of pigs and humans; wine and pigs (with a distinction between Muslims and dhimmis); bees, silkworms, human waste (except when mixed with soil), musical instruments (according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and certain uncertain things like mulaeqih (what is in the womb) and madamin (what is in the back). The passage then engages in a critical discussion of a concession from al-Qunyah regarding the sale of patents (bar'aat) and the custom of taking items like lentils, concluding that such concessions are weak. It also addresses ownership and delivery: the seller must own the item, and it must be capable of delivery. Exceptions include salam (advance sale) and usurped goods when the usurper guarantees value. The passage concludes by summarizing the conditions for the validity of a sale contract: eleven conditions (later corrected to nine) related to parties, contract, place, and subject matter, and conditions for executory power (ownership or guardianship).

Key Terms
فضولي (fuduli): A person who sells property without being the owner or having any authorization; such a sale is suspended until ratified.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct number of conditions for the conclusion (in'iqad) of a sale?
A) Eleven
B) Nine
C) Six
D) Two

Key Takeaway
Special cases (bees, patents) require careful analysis, and the conditions for a valid sale are nine for conclusion plus ownership/guardianship for execution.
