Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Rulings on Selling Non-Commodities and Unowned Items
Listing items that cannot be sold due to lack of commodity status or ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify items that cannot be sold due to lack of commodity status or ownership, and explain the Hanafi reasoning for each category.

Core Idea
A sale is invalid (bāṭil) if the item is not a legal commodity or is not owned by the seller.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq lists various items that cannot be validly sold under Hanafi fiqh. The reasons fall into two main categories: the item is not a legal commodity (ghayr mutaqawwim) like wine, or the seller does not own the item (e.g., unowned grass, water, or minerals still in the earth). Understanding these limits ensures that contracts are valid and avoid ribā and gharar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the sale of specific items. For each item, the rulings are that the sale is not concluded (lam yan'aqid) or invalid (lā yaṣiḥḥ). Determine the general ruling: all these sales are bāṭil.
2
Categorize the reason for invalidity
Classify each item into one of three reasons: (a) not a legal commodity (ghayr mutaqawwim) due to textual prohibition, (b) not owned by the seller because it is mubāḥ (ownerless) or not yet acquired, or (c) the seller does not own the right being sold (e.g., shares before receipt).
Wine falls under (a); grass, water, game, and unextracted minerals under (b); barā'āt and shares of imams under (c).
3
Examine the mining case closely
The passage discusses a person who digs a mine: he owns only what he extracts. The remaining stone in the ground remains mubāḥ. Therefore, selling the entire dig or a portion yet to be extracted is invalid because the seller does not own it.
This case also illustrates the principle that ownership of land does not automatically grant ownership of all minerals beneath it.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Some imams of Bukhara permitted the sale of shares of imams (waqf allocations) before receipt, analogizing to established waqf practices. The author (Ibn Nujaym) rejects this because ownership is not established until actual receipt. Also, a reckless mufti incorrectly allowed the heirs of a digger to prevent others from using remaining stone.
The relied-upon position is that such sales are invalid due to lack of ownership.
5
Apply the principles to new scenarios
Using the categories above, test new cases: selling grass before cutting, selling water from a river, selling land of Mecca, etc. Determine the ruling and justify using the conditions of mutaqawwim and ownership.
Always check if the item is owned and if it is a legally recognized commodity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو بمثله أو بقيمته، والخمر يجب اجتنابها بالنص فلم تكن متقومة اهـ. وفي القنية: أدنى القيمة التي تشترط لجواز البيع فلس ولو كانت كسرة خبز لا يجوز شراء البراءات التي يكتبها الديوان على العمال لا يصح . قيل له : أئمة بخارى جوزوا بيع حظوظ الأئمة . قال : لأن مال الوقف قائم ثمة ولا كذلك هنا ا هـ . فعلى هذا يجوز للمستحق في المدارس بيع خبزه قبل قبضه من المشرف بخلاف الجندي إذا باع الشعير المعين لعلف دابته قبل قبضه. وخرج الكلأ ولو في أرض مملوكة له، والماء في نهره أو في ما لا يملكه فلم ينعقد بيع بيع الصيد والحطب والحشيش قبل الإحراز وبيع أرض مكة عند الإمام، وأرض أحياها بغير إذن الإمام عند الإمام، وحوانيت السوق التي عليها غلة للسلطان لعدم الملك لأن السلطان إنما أذن لهم في البناء ولم يجعل البقعة لهم كما في البدائع. وفي القنية: حفر بالمملوك بئره، وبيع موضعاً من المعدن ثم باع تلك الحفيرة أو أجرها لا يصح لأنه إنما ملك من المعدن ما يخرج ويؤخذ وما بقي فيه بقي على الإباحة قال رضي الله تعالى عنه : وهذه رواية في واقعة بلغتني عن بعض المفتين المجازفين أنه أفتى فيمن حفر في جبل حجراً يتخذ منه القدور ثم مات ونحت غيره منه قدوراً بأن لورثة الحافر المنع تاب الله عليه وعلينا وهداه وإيانا، والصواب ليس لهم المنع لأن الحجر الباقي وإن ظهر بحفره بقي على أصل الإباحة اهـ.
Or with its like or its value. And wine must be avoided by text, so it is not a commodity. End. And in al-Qunyah: The minimum value required for the permissibility of sale is a fils, even if it is a piece of bread. Purchasing the barā'āt (bonds) that the dīwān writes on the workers is not valid. It was said to him: The imams of Bukhara permitted the sale of shares of the imams. He said: Because the waqf property is established there, but not here. End. So on this basis, it is permissible for the beneficiary in the schools to sell his bread before receiving it from the supervisor, unlike the soldier when he sells the specific barley for his animal's fodder before receiving it. And excluded: grass, even if in his owned land, and water in his river or in what he does not own, so the sale of it is not concluded; the sale of game, firewood, and grass before acquisition; and the sale of the land of Mecca according to Imam (Abu Hanifa); and land revived without the Imam's permission according to Imam (Abu Hanifa); and the shops of the market that have rental income for the sultan, due to lack of ownership, because the sultan only permitted them to build and did not make the plot for them, as in al-Badā'i'. And in al-Qunyah: [If] a free person digs his well, and sells the extracted stone from a mine, then sells that dig or rents it, it is not valid, because he only owns from the mine what is extracted and taken, and what remains remains on permissibility. He (may Allah be pleased with him) said: This is a narration concerning an incident that reached me about some reckless muftis who gave a fatwa that if someone dug a stone in a mountain to make pots from it, then died, and another carved pots from it after him, that the heirs of the first digger could prevent him—may Allah relent upon him and us and guide him and us—but the correct view is that they cannot prevent him, because the remaining stone, even if it became apparent by his digging, remains on original permissibility. End.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq, expanding on the conditions for a valid sale. It first mentions that wine is not a commodity (ghayr mutaqawwim) because it must be avoided by nass (Qur'anic text), so it cannot be the subject of a valid sale. Then it cites al-Qunyah that the minimum value for a sale to be valid is a fils, even a piece of bread. It then discusses various cases where sale is invalid due to lack of ownership or lack of commodity status: bonds written on workers (barā'āt) are not valid to buy because the worker does not own the salary until delivery; the imams of Bukhara permitted selling shares of imams because the waqf property is established there, unlike elsewhere. Based on this, a student entitled to bread from a school can sell it before receipt, but a soldier cannot sell the specific barley before receipt. Then it lists items that are not valid subjects of sale because they are not owned: grass (even on one's own land), water (in a river or unowned), game, firewood, and grass before acquisition; land of Mecca (according to Abu Hanifa) and land revived without Imam's permission; shops on sultan's land because ownership is not granted. Finally, it discusses mining: a person who digs a well owns only what he extracts; the remaining material remains mubāḥ (permissible for anyone). A fatwa claiming heirs could prevent another from using the same stone is rejected; the correct view is that the stone remains mubāḥ.

Key Terms
متقوم (mutaqawwim): A commodity that has legal value and can be the subject of a valid sale.
الإباحة (al-ibāḥah): Permissibility; here referring to the original state of being ownerless and available for anyone to take.
حظوظ الأئمة (ḥuẓūẓ al-a'immah): Shares or stipends of the imams (religious leaders) from waqf or government allocations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the sale of wine invalid according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because wine is najis (ritually impure)
B) Because wine must be avoided by nass (text), so it is not a commodity (mutaqawwim)
C) Because wine is harmful to health
D) Because wine is not owned by anyone

Key Takeaway
A sale is valid only if the item is a legal commodity (mutaqawwim) and owned by the seller; otherwise it is bāṭil.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Conditions of Nafadh: Ownership and Guardianship
Ruling on effectiveness conditions for sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for a sale to be effective (nafadh) and determine the ruling on a sale by an unauthorized person (fuduli) and by a minor.

Core Idea
A sale's effectiveness depends on the seller's ownership or guardianship; without these, it is suspended (mawqūf).

The Hanafi school distinguishes between a sale being concluded (in‘iqād) and being effective (nafadh). This lesson explores the first category of conditions: those required for the sale to produce its immediate legal effects. Understanding who can sell and under what authority is foundational to Islamic contract law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions for nafadh
From the text: 'ownership or guardianship.' A sale without either is not effective.
2
Examine the ruling on a fuduli sale
A sale by an unauthorized person (fuduli) is not effective; it is suspended (mawqūf) awaiting ratification. However, the fuduli's purchase as buyer is effective immediately.
3
List the hierarchy of guardians in property
The text provides a clear order: father, his executor, then the executor's executor, grandfather, etc., ending with the judge. No one else (mother, brother, uncle) has guardianship.
4
Determine the ruling on a minor's sale
An intelligent minor's sale is suspended if he is interdicted (maḥjūr) and effective if permitted (ma’dhūn).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما شرائط النفاذ فالملك أو الولاية فلم ينعقد بيع الفضولي عندنا، وأما شراؤه فنافذ كما سيأتي، والولاية إما بإنابة المالك أو الشارع. فالأول الوكالة والثاني ولاية الأب ومن قام مقامه بشرط إسلام الولي وحريته وعقله وبلوغه وصغر المولى عليه. وأولى الأولياء في المال الأب ثم وصيه ثم وصي وصيه ثم الجد أبو الأب ثم وصيه ثم وصي وصيه ثم القاضي ثم من نصبه القاضي، وليس لمن سواهم ولاية في المال من الأم والأخ والعم، ولوصيهم ولاية المنقول للحفظ والعقار لقضاء دين الميت خاصة وليس له التصرف. وأما وصي المكاتب فلا يملك الاقضاء دين المكاتب فيبيع له ولا يملك بعده إلا الحفظ في رواية الزيادات، وفي رواية كتاب القسمة جعله كوصي الأب. هذا إذا مات قبل الأداء، وأما بعده فوصيه كوصي الأحرار فانعقد بيع الصبي العاقل عندنا موقوفاً إن كان محجوراً، ونافذاً إن كان مأذوناً. الثاني أن لا يكون في المبيع حق لغير البائع، فإن كان لا ينفذ كالمرهون والمستأجر واختلفت عبارات الكتب فيها ففي بعضها أنه فاسد، والصحيح أنه موقوف ويحمل الفساد على أنه لا حكم له ظاهراً وهو تفسير الموقوف عندنا ويملكان الإجازة دون الفسخ ويفسخه المشتري إن لم يعلم به أولاً، وأما بيع عبد وجب عليه قود فنافذ كبيع المرتد والجاني ومن وجب عليه حد. وأما شرائط الصحة فعامة وخاصة ؛ والعامة لكل ما هو شرط الانعقاد لأن ما لا بيع ينعقد لم يصح ولا ينعكس فإن الفاسد عندنا منعقد نافذ إذا اتصل به القبض. ومنها أن لا يكون مؤقتاً فإن أقته لم يصح بخلاف الإجارة فإن التأقيت شرطها. ومنها أن يكون المبيع معلوماً والثمن معلوماً علماً يمنع من المنازعة فالمجهول جهالة مفضية إليها غير صحيح القطيع، وبيع الشيء بقيمته ويحكم فلان ومنها خلوه عن شرط مفسد وهو أنواع شرط في وجوده غرر كاشتراط حمل البهيمة واختلفت الروايات في اشتراط حمل الجارية، ورجح بعضهم أن الشارط له إن كان البائع صح وكان تبرياً . منه وإن كان المشتري ليتخذها ظئراً فسد. ومنه إذا اشترى كبشاً على أنه نطاح ومنه شرط لا يقتضيه العقد وفيه منفعة لأحديهما وسيأتي تفصيله ومنه شرط الأجل في المبيع المعين والثمن المعين وإنما يجوز في الدين. ومنه شرط خيار مؤبد، ومنه شرط خيار مؤقت مجهول، ومنه شرط خیار مطلق من هذا كشاة والصحيح خلافها قوله : (وصغر المولى عليه يرد على التقييد المجنون. قوله : (الثاني أن لا يكون في المبيع حق لغير البائع أي الثاني من شرائط النفاذ والأول هو قوله الملك أو الولاية. قوله : (كالمرهون والمستأجر) قال الرملي قال في الخانية : فإن أراد المستأجر أن يفسخ البيع اختلفوا فيه والصحيح أنه لا يملك الفسخ. وفي الزيلعي في بيع المرهون وفي أصح الروايتين لا ينفسخ بفسخه. ومثله في الكافي والهداية والجوهرة وأكثر الكتب المعتبرة فكان عليه المعول وعبارة الكافي صريحة في أن القاضي لا يملك الفسخ بدون طلب المشتري قال بعد ذكر ما تقدم من عدم جواز فسخ الراهن : والمستأجر والمشتري بالخيار إن شاء صبر حتى يفتك الرهن وإن شاء رفع الأمر إلى القاضي ليفسخ بحكم العجز عن التسليم إذ ولاية الفسخ للقاضي لا إليه قوله : (ولا ينعكس) أي بأن يقال ما لا
As for the conditions of effectiveness (nafādh): ownership or guardianship; so the sale by an unauthorized person (fudūlī) is not effective according to us, but his purchase is effective as will come. And guardianship is either by appointment of the owner or by the Lawgiver. The first is agency, the second is the guardianship of the father and whoever stands in his place, conditioned on the guardian being Muslim, free, sane, adult, and the ward being a minor. The foremost guardians in property are: the father, then his executor, then the executor of his executor, then the grandfather (father's father), then his executor, then the executor of his executor, then the judge, then whomever the judge appoints. No one else has guardianship in property: not the mother, brother, or paternal uncle. And their executor has guardianship over movable property for preservation and over real estate specifically for paying the debt of the deceased; he has no right of disposition. As for the executor of a mukātab (slave who contracts for freedom), he does not have the right to pay the debt of the mukātab, so he sells for him; and after that he has no right except preservation, in one narration from al-Ziyādāt. In another narration from Kitāb al-Qisma, he is like the executor of the father. This is if the mukātab dies before payment; but after payment, his executor is like the executor of a free person. So the sale by an intelligent minor (ṣabī ʿāqil) is concluded as suspended (mawqūf) according to us if he is interdicted, and effective if he is permitted. Second: that there be no right of another in the sold item; if there is, it is not effective, like a mortgaged item or leased item. The expressions of the books differ; in some it is corrupt (fāsid), but the correct view is that it is suspended (mawqūf) and 'corrupt' is interpreted as having no apparent legal effect, which is the interpretation of 'suspended' in our school. Both (mortgagor and lessor) have the authority to ratify but not to rescind; the buyer may rescind if he was initially unaware. As for the sale of a slave upon whom qawad (retaliation) is due, it is effective, like the sale of an apostate, a criminal, and one upon whom a ḥadd punishment is due. As for the conditions of soundness (ṣiḥḥa): they are general and specific. The general conditions are everything that is a condition for validity (inʿiqād), because any sale that is not concluded cannot be sound, but the converse does not hold, because the corrupt (fāsid) sale according to us is concluded and effective if delivery is connected to it. Among them: that it not be temporary; if he fixes a time, it is not sound, unlike ijarah where fixing a time is a condition. Among them: that the sold item be known and the price be known with knowledge that prevents dispute; what is unknown with ignorance leading to dispute is not sound, like selling a pregnant sheep and the like, or selling something at its value or by the judgment of so-and-so. Among them: freedom from a corrupt condition, which is of several types: a condition involving uncertainty (gharar) like stipulating the offspring of an animal; the narrations differ on stipulating the offspring of a slave-girl; some preponderated that if the stipulator is the seller, it is sound and is a disclaimer; if it is the buyer to use her as a wet nurse, it becomes corrupt. Among them: if he buys a ram on condition it butts. Among them: a condition not required by the contract that has a benefit for one of the parties, details to come. Among them: a condition of deferment in the specific sold item and specific price; it is only permissible in debt. Among them: a condition of perpetual option, a condition of temporary option that is unspecified, a condition of absolute option (like 'from this sheep'?). The sound view is the opposite of this. His statement: 'and the ward being a minor' is a restriction that applies also to the insane. His statement: 'Second: that there be no right of another in the sold item' means the second of the conditions of effectiveness, the first being ownership or guardianship. His statement: 'like mortgaged and leased' - al-Ramli said in al-Khāniyya: if the lessor wants to rescind the sale, they differed; the correct view is that he does not have the right to rescind. And in al-Zaylaʿī on the sale of a mortgaged item: in the soundest of the two narrations, it is not rescinded by his rescission. Similar is in al-Kāfī, al-Hidāya, al-Jawhara, and most reliable books, so it is relied upon. The expression of al-Kāfī is explicit that the judge does not have the right to rescind without the request of the buyer; after mentioning the preceding that the mortgagor cannot rescind: 'and the lessor and the buyer have the option: if they wish, they may wait until the mortgage is redeemed, and if they wish, they may raise the matter to the judge to rescind due to inability to deliver, since the authority of rescission belongs to the judge, not to him.' His statement: 'and it does not reverse' means that it is not said that what does not...
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage covers the conditions for a sale to be effective (nāfidh) and sound (ṣaḥīḥ). The author first explains that effectiveness requires either the seller's ownership or legal guardianship. A sale by an unauthorized person (fudūlī) is not effective but is suspended; however, his purchase (as buyer) is effective. Guardianship types include agency (from owner) and legal guardianship (from Shari'ah). The order of guardians in property is listed: father, his executor, then the executor's executor, grandfather, etc., then judge. Others like mother, brother, uncle have no guardianship over property. Executors have limited powers. The sale by an intelligent minor is suspended if he is under interdiction (maḥjūr), but effective if permitted (maʾdhūn). Another condition: the sold item must not have another's right attached (like mortgage or lease); such sales are suspended, not corrupt, and the mortgagee/lessee may ratify but not rescind; the buyer can rescind if unaware. Exceptions: sale of a slave subject to qawad (retaliation) is effective. Then conditions of soundness (ṣiḥḥa) are discussed: general conditions include all conditions of inʿiqād, but a fāsid sale can be effective if delivery occurs. Specific conditions: no time limit (unlike ijarah), knowledge of item and price (avoiding ignorance leading to dispute), and freedom from corrupt conditions. Corrupt conditions include: gharar (e.g., stipulating offspring of animal), condition not required by contract with benefit for one party, deferment in spot sale, perpetual or indefinite option, etc. The author also includes glosses from commentators: the condition of ward being a minor also covers the insane; the second condition of nafādh is that no third-party right exists; there is disagreement on whether the lessor can rescind the sale, but correct view is no; the judge may rescind at the buyer's request due to inability to deliver. The passage ends mid-sentence explaining 'and it does not reverse'.

Key Terms
نافذ (nāfidh): Effective; a sale that produces its legal effects immediately without being suspended.
موقوف (mawqūf): Suspended; a sale whose legal effects depend on ratification by the rightful party (e.g., owner or guardian).
ولي (walī): Guardian; a person legally entitled to manage the property of another (e.g., father, executor, judge).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a sale by an unauthorized person (fuduli)?
A) The sale is bāṭil (void) and cannot be corrected.
B) The sale is mawqūf (suspended) and becomes effective upon ratification by the owner.
C) The sale is nāfidh (effective) if the buyer is unaware.
D) The sale is fāsid (corrupt) and must be remade.

Key Takeaway
A sale's effectiveness hinges on the seller's ownership or guardianship; without these, the sale is suspended, not void.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
advanced
10 min
Sales Involving Third-Party Rights: Mortgage and Lease
Refining the ruling on sales with third-party rights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a sale of a mortgaged or leased item as mawqūf and explain the rights of ratification and rescission.

Core Idea
A sale that affects another's right is suspended, not corrupt; only the buyer can seek rescission through the judge.

When the sold item is already subject to another's right (like mortgage or lease), the sale's effectiveness is impacted. The Hanafi school treats such sales as suspended (mawqūf) rather than void, allowing the third party to ratify or the buyer to seek judicial rescission. This lesson examines the details.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second condition of nafadh
The text states: 'there be no right of another in the sold item.' If such a right exists, the sale is not effective.
2
Determine the correct ruling: fāsid or mawqūf?
Some books call it fāsid, but the correct Hanafi view is mawqūf. 'Fāsid' here is interpreted as having no apparent effect, which is the same as mawqūf.
3
Identify who can ratify and who can rescind
The mortgagee (creditor) or lessor can ratify the sale but cannot rescind it. The buyer, if unaware of the third-party right, may rescind by going to the judge.
4
Note exceptions: slave with qawad or hadd
A sale of a slave subject to qawad (retaliation) or hadd penalty is effective, not suspended, because the right is not a property right.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

مستأجر (musta’jir): Lessee; one who has the right to use the item for a period.
إجازة (ijāzah): Ratification; subsequent approval that makes a suspended contract effective.

In the Hanafi school, who has the right to rescind a sale of a mortgaged item?

Conditions of Soundness (Sihha) in Sales
Ruling on conditions of soundness and corrupt conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the general and specific conditions of soundness (sihha) in a sale and distinguish between corrupt conditions (shurūṭ mufsida) and the concept of fāsid.

Core Idea
A sale is sound only if the item and price are known, it is not temporary, and it is free from corrupt conditions.

Beyond effectiveness, a sale must also be sound (ṣaḥīḥ) in the sense of being free from defects that affect its validity. The Hanafi school outlines conditions that must be met for a sale to be considered ṣaḥīḥ, including knowledge of the subject matter, absence of time restrictions, and freedom from corrupt conditions that introduce uncertainty or unjust enrichment.

Guided Path
1
State the general condition of sihha
The text: 'the general conditions are everything that is a condition for in‘iqād (conclusion).' Any sale that is not concluded cannot be sound.
2
List the specific conditions of sihha
The text mentions: (1) the sale must not be temporary, (2) the item and price must be known (avoiding ignorance that leads to dispute), (3) freedom from corrupt conditions.
3
Classify examples of corrupt conditions
Corrupt conditions include: gharar (stipulating offspring of an animal), benefit for one party not required by contract, deferment in a spot sale of specific item, perpetual or indefinite option.
4
Understand the ruling on a fāsid sale
A fāsid sale is concluded (mun‘aqid) and can become effective if delivery occurs and the corrupt condition is removed. It is not bāṭil.

ما لا
As for the conditions of effectiveness (nafādh): ownership or guardianship; so the sale by an unauthorized person (fudūlī) is not effective according to us, but his purchase is effective as will come. And guardianship is either by appointment of the owner or by the Lawgiver. The first is agency, the second is the guardianship of the father and whoever stands in his place, conditioned on the guardian being Muslim, free, sane, adult, and the ward being a minor. The foremost guardians in property are: the father, then his executor, then the executor of his executor, then the grandfather (father's father), then his executor, then the executor of his executor, then the judge, then whomever the judge appoints. No one else has guardianship in property: not the mother, brother, or paternal uncle. And their executor has guardianship over movable property for preservation and over real estate specifically for paying the debt of the deceased; he has no right of disposition. As for the executor of a mukātab (slave who contracts for freedom), he does not have the right to pay the debt of the mukātab, so he sells for him; and after that he has no right except preservation, in one narration from al-Ziyādāt. In another narration from Kitāb al-Qisma, he is like the executor of the father. This is if the mukātab dies before payment; but after payment, his executor is like the executor of a free person. So the sale by an intelligent minor (ṣabī ʿāqil) is concluded as suspended (mawqūf) according to us if he is interdicted, and effective if he is permitted. Second: that there be no right of another in the sold item; if there is, it is not effective, like a mortgaged item or leased item. The expressions of the books differ; in some it is corrupt (fāsid), but the correct view is that it is suspended (mawqūf) and 'corrupt' is interpreted as having no apparent legal effect, which is the interpretation of 'suspended' in our school. Both (mortgagor and lessor) have the authority to ratify but not to rescind; the buyer may rescind if he was initially unaware. As for the sale of a slave upon whom qawad (retaliation) is due, it is effective, like the sale of an apostate, a criminal, and one upon whom a ḥadd punishment is due. As for the conditions of soundness (ṣiḥḥa): they are general and specific. The general conditions are everything that is a condition for validity (inʿiqād), because any sale that is not concluded cannot be sound, but the converse does not hold, because the corrupt (fāsid) sale according to us is concluded and effective if delivery is connected to it. Among them: that it not be temporary; if he fixes a time, it is not sound, unlike ijarah where fixing a time is a condition. Among them: that the sold item be known and the price be known with knowledge that prevents dispute; what is unknown with ignorance leading to dispute is not sound, like selling a pregnant sheep and the like, or selling something at its value or by the judgment of so-and-so. Among them: freedom from a corrupt condition, which is of several types: a condition involving uncertainty (gharar) like stipulating the offspring of an animal; the narrations differ on stipulating the offspring of a slave-girl; some preponderated that if the stipulator is the seller, it is sound and is a disclaimer; if it is the buyer to use her as a wet nurse, it becomes corrupt. Among them: if he buys a ram on condition it butts. Among them: a condition not required by the contract that has a benefit for one of the parties, details to come. Among them: a condition of deferment in the specific sold item and specific price; it is only permissible in debt. Among them: a condition of perpetual option, a condition of temporary option that is unspecified, a condition of absolute option (like 'from this sheep'?). The sound view is the opposite of this. His statement: 'and the ward being a minor' is a restriction that applies also to the insane. His statement: 'Second: that there be no right of another in the sold item' means the second of the conditions of effectiveness, the first being ownership or guardianship. His statement: 'like mortgaged and leased' - al-Ramli said in al-Khāniyya: if the lessor wants to rescind the sale, they differed; the correct view is that he does not have the right to rescind. And in al-Zaylaʿī on the sale of a mortgaged item: in the soundest of the two narrations, it is not rescinded by his rescission. Similar is in al-Kāfī, al-Hidāya, al-Jawhara, and most reliable books, so it is relied upon. The expression of al-Kāfī is explicit that the judge does not have the right to rescind without the request of the buyer; after mentioning the preceding that the mortgagor cannot rescind: 'and the lessor and the buyer have the option: if they wish, they may wait until the mortgage is redeemed, and if they wish, they may raise the matter to the judge to rescind due to inability to deliver, since the authority of rescission belongs to the judge, not to him.' His statement: 'and it does not reverse' means that it is not said that what does not...

Commentary
This passage covers the conditions for a sale to be effective (nāfidh) and sound (ṣaḥīḥ). The author first explains that effectiveness requires either the seller's ownership or legal guardianship. A sale by an unauthorized person (fudūlī) is not effective but is suspended; however, his purchase (as buyer) is effective. Guardianship types include agency (from owner) and legal guardianship (from Shari'ah). The order of guardians in property is listed: father, his executor, then the executor's executor, grandfather, etc., then judge. Others like mother, brother, uncle have no guardianship over property. Executors have limited powers. The sale by an intelligent minor is suspended if he is under interdiction (maḥjūr), but effective if permitted (maʾdhūn). Another condition: the sold item must not have another's right attached (like mortgage or lease); such sales are suspended, not corrupt, and the mortgagee/lessee may ratify but not rescind; the buyer can rescind if unaware. Exceptions: sale of a slave subject to qawad (retaliation) is effective. Then conditions of soundness (ṣiḥḥa) are discussed: general conditions include all conditions of inʿiqād, but a fāsid sale can be effective if delivery occurs. Specific conditions: no time limit (unlike ijarah), knowledge of item and price (avoiding ignorance leading to dispute), and freedom from corrupt conditions. Corrupt conditions include: gharar (e.g., stipulating offspring of animal), condition not required by contract with benefit for one party, deferment in spot sale, perpetual or indefinite option, etc. The author also includes glosses from commentators: the condition of ward being a minor also covers the insane; the second condition of nafādh is that no third-party right exists; there is disagreement on whether the lessor can rescind the sale, but correct view is no; the judge may rescind at the buyer's request due to inability to deliver. The passage ends mid-sentence explaining 'and it does not reverse'.

Key Terms
شرط مفسد (sharṭ mufsid): A corrupt condition that renders the sale fāsid, such as gharar, benefit for one party, or indefinite option.
غرر (gharar): Uncertainty or risk in the subject matter or price, which can lead to dispute.
جاهلة مفضية إلى نزاع (jāhilah mufdiya ilā nizā‘): Ignorance that leads to dispute; the item or price must be known to avoid this.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a sale where the seller stipulates that the buyer must not resell the item for a month?
A) The sale is valid because the condition is temporary and reasonable.
B) The sale is fāsid (corrupt) because the condition provides benefit to the seller not required by the contract.
C) The sale is bāṭil (void) because it restricts the buyer's ownership.
D) The sale is mawqūf (suspended) until the condition is fulfilled.

Key Takeaway
A sale's soundness requires knowledge, no time limit, and freedom from corrupt conditions; a fāsid sale is not void but can be remedied.

Guardianship and Sale of a Minor's Property in Hanafi Fiqh
Explanation of guardian hierarchy and ruling on minor's sale with intra-Hanafi riwaya difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi guardian hierarchy for a minor's property, explain the conditions for guardianship, and distinguish between the rulings on a minor's sale (nafidh vs mawquf) and identify the intra-Hanafi difference regarding the executor of a mukatib.

Core Idea
A minor's sale is valid but suspended if interdicted; guardian hierarchy is strict, and the executor of a mukatib has limited powers differing by riwaya.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq details the Hanafi rules of guardianship over a minor's property. It lays out the strict hierarchy of guardians, the conditions required, and the limitations on an executor's authority. It also addresses the important distinction between a discerning minor's sale and an insane person's, and presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the executor of a mukatib (a slave contracting freedom).

Guided Path
1
Identify Conditions and Hierarchy
Read from the beginning to the list of guardians. Note the conditions for the guardian (Muslim, free, sane, adult) and the ward (minor). Then list the order of priority: father, his executor, executor's executor, paternal grandfather, his executor, his executor's executor, judge, judge's appointee.
No other relatives like mother or uncle have authority in property.
2
Analyze Executor Powers
The passage states that an executor has limited powers: only preservation of moveable property and using real estate to pay the deceased's debt. He cannot freely dispose.
This restriction is important to prevent misuse.
3
Examine the Mukatib Dispute
Focus on the two narrations (riwayat): in al-Ziyadat, the executor of a mukatib cannot pay the mukatib's debt; in Kitab al-Qisma, he is like the father's executor and can pay. Understand the practical difference.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; both are recorded without explicit tarjih.
4
Derive the Ruling on Minor's Sale
The passage concludes that the sale of a discerning minor is mawquf (suspended) if the minor is mahjur (interdicted), and nafidh (valid and effective) if madhun (permitted). Understand why sale of an insane person is invalid.
The word 'minor' excludes the insane, as they lack discernment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقامه بشرط إسلام الولي وحريته وعقله وبلوغه وصغر المولى عليه. وأولى الأولياء في المال الأب ثم وصيه ثم وصي وصيه ثم الجد أبو الأب ثم وصيه ثم وصي وصيه ثم القاضي ثم من نصبه القاضي، وليس لمن سواهم ولاية في المال من الأم والأخ والعم، ولوصيهم ولاية المنقول للحفظ والعقار لقضاء دين الميت خاصة وليس له التصرف. وأما وصي المكاتب فلا يملك الاقضاء دين المكاتب فيبيع له ولا يملك بعده إلا الحفظ في رواية الزيادات، وفي رواية كتاب القسمة جعله كوصي الأب. هذا إذا مات قبل الأداء، وأما بعده فوصيه كوصي الأحرار فانعقد بيع الصبي العاقل عندنا موقوفاً إن كان محجوراً، ونافذاً إن كان مأذوناً. قوله : (وصغر المولى عليه يرد على التقييد المجنون.
Its place requires the condition of the guardian being Muslim, free, sane, adult, and the ward being minor. The first of guardians regarding property is the father, then his executor, then the executor's executor, then the paternal grandfather, then his executor, then his executor's executor, then the judge, then the one appointed by the judge. No one else has authority over property from the mother, brother, or paternal uncle. The executor has authority over movable property for preservation and over real estate for paying the deceased's debt specifically, but he does not have the right to dispose. As for the executor of a mukatib, he does not have the right to pay the mukatib's debt; he sells for him and thereafter has only custody per the narration of al-Ziyadat, and in the narration of Kitab al-Qisma he is made like the father's executor. This is if he dies before payment; after payment, his executor is like the executor of free persons. Thus the sale of a discerning minor is, in our view, suspended if he is interdicted, and valid if he is permitted. He said: (And the ward being minor) refutes the restriction to the insane.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with intra-Hanafi dispute. It sets out the conditions for guardianship in the sale of a minor's property: the guardian must be Muslim, free, sane, and adult; the ward must be minor. The order of priority for guardians is given: father, his executor, that executor's executor, then grandfather, his executor, his executor's executor, then the judge, then the judge's appointee. No other relatives (mother, brother, uncle) have authority. The executor's authority is limited: he can only preserve movable property and use real estate to pay the deceased's debts. For a mukatib (a slave contracting freedom), the executor's powers differ by riwaya: in al-Ziyadat he cannot pay the debt; in Kitab al-Qisma he is like the father's executor. This applies if the mukatib dies before payment; after payment, the executor is like that of free persons. The central ruling: a discerning minor's sale is mawquf (suspended) if he is interdicted (mahjur), and nafidh (enforceable) if he is permitted (madhun). The gloss clarifies that the condition 'minor' excludes the insane, as insanity is a separate incapacity.

Key Terms
الولي (al-wali): The guardian who has authority over the minor's property; conditions: Muslim, free, sane, adult.
المولى عليه (al-mawla 'alayhi): The ward under guardianship, specifically a minor.
مكاتب (mukatib): A slave who has a contract to earn his freedom by paying installments.
البيع موقوف (al-bay' mawquf): A sale that is valid but its effect is suspended pending ratification by the guardian.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The sale is invalid (batil) and cannot be ratified.
B) The sale is suspended (mawquf) and becomes valid upon guardian's ratification.
C) The sale is valid (nafidh) without any condition.
D) The sale is void (fasid) and must be repeated.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a discerning minor's property transaction is valid but suspended if the minor is interdicted, and fully effective if permitted; the guardian hierarchy is strict, and the executor of a mukatib has varying authority per riwaya.

Condition: No Third-Party Right in Sold Item
Explanation of the second condition for enforceability of sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling mortgaged or leased property, explain why it is classified as mawquf (suspended) rather than fasid, and identify who has the authority to annul such a sale.

Core Idea
A sale of property with a third-party right is valid but suspended — its enforceability depends on ratification, and the right-holder cannot unilaterally annul.

We now examine the second condition for a sale to be enforceable (nafadh): that the sold item must not carry a right belonging to anyone other than the seller. This condition complements the first condition — ownership or authority — and ensures that the sale does not conflict with existing legal interests. In this lesson, we explore how the Hanafi school treats sales of mortgaged or leased property, where a third party (the creditor or lessee) holds a right that appears to conflict with the buyer's ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and its place
The passage states that the second condition for enforceability is that the sold item be free of any right belonging to a non-seller. Recognize that this is a condition for nafadh, not for validity (siḥḥa).
The first condition was ownership or authority (milk or wilāya).
2
Examine the examples cited
The author gives two examples: mortgaged property (al-marhūn) and leased property (al-musta’jar). In both, a third party (creditor or lessee) has a right that attaches to the item.
These are cases where the third party's right prevents immediate delivery or full ownership.
3
Determine the correct Hanafi ruling
The passage clarifies that such a sale is not fasid (invalid) but rather mawquf (suspended). It is sahīh (valid) in its contract but its effect (nafadh) depends on ratification by the right-holder or the buyer.
Earlier books mistakenly called it fasid, but the reliable books (al-Kāfī, al-Hidāyah, al-Jawharah) confirm it is mawquf.
4
Understand the authority to annul
The lessee or mortgagor cannot unilaterally annul the sale. Only the judge has the power to annul, and only if the buyer requests it due to inability to deliver the item free of the third-party right.
The buyer has an option (khiyār): wait for the encumbrance to be removed or seek judicial rescission.
5
Resolve the linguistic nuance
The phrase 'it does not reverse' (لا ينعكس) means that not everything that falls short of a condition is automatically invalid. For example, the sale of a coerced person (al-mukrah) is fasid but still 'concluded' (mun‘aqid) though suspended. The author notes a terminological imprecision in calling it 'concluded and enforceable'.
The author suggests 'concluded and possessed' (mun‘aqid mamlūk) as more accurate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (الثاني أن لا يكون في المبيع حق لغير البائع أي الثاني من شرائط النفاذ والأول هو قوله الملك أو الولاية. قوله : (كالمرهون والمستأجر) قال الرملي قال في الخانية : فإن أراد المستأجر أن يفسخ البيع اختلفوا فيه والصحيح أنه لا يملك الفسخ. وفي الزيلعي في بيع المرهون وفي أصح الروايتين لا ينفسخ بفسخه. ومثله في الكافي والهداية والجوهرة وأكثر الكتب المعتبرة فكان عليه المعول وعبارة الكافي صريحة في أن القاضي لا يملك الفسخ بدون طلب المشتري قال بعد ذكر ما تقدم من عدم جواز فسخ الراهن : والمستأجر والمشتري بالخيار إن شاء صبر حتى يفتك الرهن وإن شاء رفع الأمر إلى القاضي ليفسخ بحكم العجز عن التسليم إذ ولاية الفسخ للقاضي لا إليه قوله : (ولا ينعكس) أي بأن يقال ما لا يصح منه منعقد كالفاسد ومنه غيره كالباطل وفي قوله «منعقد نافذ» نظر فإن بيع المكره من الفاسد كما قدمه وهو منعقد موقوف وكان الظاهر أن يقول منعقد مملوك تأمل .
His saying: (Second, that there be no right of another in the sold item) meaning the second of the conditions of enforceability, the first being his saying: ownership or authority. His saying: (like the mortgaged and the leased) — al-Ramli said in al-Khaniyah: If the lessee wants to annul the sale, they differed; the correct view is that he cannot annul. And in al-Zaylai on the sale of the mortgaged item: in the more correct of the two narrations, it is not annulled by his annulment. Similar is in al-Kafi, al-Hidayah, al-Jawharah, and most reliable books, so reliance is upon it. The wording of al-Kafi is explicit that the judge cannot annul without the buyer's request — after mentioning the aforementioned impossibility of the mortgagor's annulment: The lessee and the buyer have the option: if they wish, they wait until the pledge is redeemed; if they wish, they refer the matter to the judge to annul due to inability to deliver, for the authority of annulment belongs to the judge, not to him. His saying: (And it does not reverse) meaning that one should not say that what is not valid is not concluded, like the invalid; and from it [the category] is other than it, like the void. And in his saying 'concluded and enforceable' there is consideration, because the sale of the coerced is from the invalid as he previously stated, and it is concluded but suspended; it should have been said 'concluded and possessed' — reflect.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the second condition for enforceability (nafadh) of a sale: that the sold item must not have a right belonging to a third party. It clarifies that the first condition is ownership or authority. The comment then addresses examples: mortgaged property (right of creditor) and leased property (right of lessee). The correct Hanafi view is that such a sale is not invalid (fasid) but suspended (mawquf), awaiting ratification. The lessee or buyer cannot unilaterally annul; the judge may annul if the buyer requests and there is inability to deliver. The discussion also clarifies a linguistic point: the phrase 'does not reverse' (لا ينعكس) means that not every sale that fails to meet a condition of validity is necessarily invalid; rather, some are valid but suspended. The author notes that calling the sale of a coerced person 'concluded and enforceable' is problematic because it is fasid and actually suspended; it should be called 'concluded and possessed'.

Key Terms
البيع الموقوف (al-bay' al-mawquf): A sale that is valid but its effect is dependent on another's ratification.
فسخ (faskh): Annulment of a contract by a party or judge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Can a lessee (مستأجر) annul the sale of a leased property without the buyer's involvement?
A) Yes, he has the right to annul anytime.
B) No, he cannot annul; the option belongs to the judge.
C) He can annul only if the buyer agrees.
D) He can annul after a specific period.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a sale of property with a third-party right is valid but suspended (mawquf), not invalid (fasid), and only a judge can annul it at the buyer's request.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
General Conditions of Validity for a Sale
Listing of general conditions of validity for a sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five general conditions of validity for a sale and identify which violations render the sale fasid.

Core Idea
A sale is valid only if it is immediate, known, free of corrupting conditions, consensual, and beneficial.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rāʾiq compiles the general conditions that must be present for a sale to be valid (ṣaḥīḥ) in the Hanafi school. Unlike specific conditions that apply only to certain types of sales, these general conditions apply to all sales. Understanding them is essential to distinguish a valid sale from an invalid (fāsid) sale and a void (bāṭil) sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first general condition: no temporariness
The passage states that a sale must not be temporary; timing it invalidates the sale, unlike rental where a term is a condition.
2
Identify the second general condition: knowledge of object and price
The sold item and price must be known sufficiently to prevent dispute; ignorance leading to dispute invalidates.
3
Identify the third general condition: freedom from invalidating conditions
Several types of invalidating conditions (shurūṭ mufsida) are listed, including those causing gharar, conditions not required by the contract that benefit one party, stipulating a time in a specific sold item (only allowed for debts), perpetual option, unknown option period, etc.
4
Identify the fourth general condition: consent
The sale of a coerced person is invalid.
5
Identify the fifth general condition: benefit in the transaction
Selling something with no benefit (e.g., a dirham for a dirham equal in weight) is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما شرائط الصحة فعامة وخاصة ; فالعامة لكل ما هو شرط الانعقاد لأن ما لا بيع ينعقد لم يصح ولا ينعكس فإن الفاسد عندنا منعقد نافذ إذا اتصل به القبض. ومنها أن لا يكون مؤقتاً فإن أقته لم يصح بخلاف الإجارة فإن التأقيت شرطها. ومنها أن يكون المبيع معلوماً والثمن معلوماً علماً يمنع من المنازعة فالمجهول جهالة مفضية إليها غير صحيح القطيع، وبيع الشيء بقيمته ويحكم فلان ومنها خلوه عن شرط مفسد وهو أنواع شرط في وجوده غرر كاشتراط حمل البهيمة واختلفت الروايات في اشتراط حمل الجارية، ورجح بعضهم أن الشارط له إن كان البائع صح وكان تبرياً . منه وإن كان المشتري ليتخذها ظئراً فسد. ومنه إذا اشترى كبشاً على أنه نطاح ومنه شرط لا يقتضيه العقد وفيه منفعة لأحديهما وسيأتي تفصيله ومنه شرط الأجل في المبيع المعين والثمن المعين وإنما يجوز في الدين. ومنه شرط خيار مؤبد، ومنه شرط خيار مؤقت مجهول، ومنه شرط خیار مطلق من هذا كشاة والصحيح خلافها قوله : (وصغر المولى عليه يرد على التقييد المجنون. قوله : (الثاني أن لا يكون في المبيع حق لغير البائع أي الثاني من شرائط النفاذ والأول هو قوله الملك أو الولاية. قوله : (كالمرهون والمستأجر) قال الرملي قال في الخانية : فإن أراد المستأجر أن يفسخ البيع اختلفوا فيه والصحيح أنه لا يملك الفسخ. وفي الزيلعي في بيع المرهون وفي أصح الروايتين لا ينفسخ بفسخه. ومثله في الكافي والهداية والجوهرة وأكثر الكتب المعتبرة فكان عليه المعول وعبارة الكافي صريحة في أن القاضي لا يملك الفسخ بدون طلب المشتري قال بعد ذكر ما تقدم من عدم جواز فسخ الراهن : والمستأجر والمشتري بالخيار إن شاء صبر حتى يفتك الرهن وإن شاء رفع الأمر إلى القاضي ليفسخ بحكم العجز عن التسليم إذ ولاية الفسخ للقاضي لا إليه قوله : (ولا ينعكس) أي بأن يقال ما لا يصح منه منعقد كالفاسد ومنه غيره كالباطل وفي قوله «منعقد نافذ» نظر فإن بيع المكره من الفاسد كما قدمه وهو منعقد موقوف وكان الظاهر أن يقول منعقد مملوك تأمل . وممنه شرط خيار مؤقت معلوم زائد على الثلاثة ومنه استثناء حمل الجارية، ومنه الرضا ففسد المكره وشراؤه وكذا البيع تلجئة ويملك الأول بالقبض دون الثاني. ومنها الفائدة بيع ما فبيع لا فائدة فيه وشراؤه فاسد، ففسد بيع درهم بدرهم استويا وزناً وصفة؛ كذا في الذخيرة. وأما الخاصة فمنها معلومية الأجل في البيع بثمن مؤجل ففسد إن كان مجهولاً، ومنها القبض في بيع المشتري المنقول وفي الدين فبيع الدين قبل قبضه فاسد كالمسلم فيه، ورأس المال ولو بعد الإقالة، وبيع شيء بالدين الذي على فلان بخلاف ما إذا كان على البائع. ومنها أن يكون البدل مسمى في أحد نوعي المبادلة وهي القولية، فإن سكت عنه فسد وملك بالقبض، وإن نفاه قيل فسد وقيل بطل فلا يملك بالقبض. وفي التتمة باعه بدين عليه وهما يعلمان أن عليه لم يصح ومنها المماثلة بين البدلين في أموال الربا وسيأتي تفصيله في بابه. ومنها الخلو عن شبهة الربا، ومنها وجود شرائط السلم الآتية، ومنها القبض في الصرف قبل الافتراق ومنها أن يكون الثمن الأول معلوماً في بيع المرابحة والتولية والإشراك والوضيعة. وأما شرائط اللزوم بعد الانعقاد والنفاذ فخلوه من الخيارات الأربعة المشهورة، ويزاد خيار الكمية وخيار الغبن إذا كان فيه غرور، وخيار استحقاق بعض المبيع القيمي مطلقاً والمثلي قبل القبض، وخيار الخيانة في المرابحة وخيار نقد الثمن ،وعدمه وخيار كشف الحال، وخيار فوات وصف مرغوب فيه، وخيار إجازة بيع الفضولي وخيار هلاك بعض المبيع فهي ثلاثة يصح لم ينعقد لأن ما لا يصح منه منعقد كالفاسد ومنه غيره كالباطل وفي قوله «منعقد نافذ» نظر فإن بيع المكره من الفاسد كما قدمه وهو منعقد موقوف وكان الظاهر أن يقول منعقد مملوك تأمل . قوله : (ومنه شرط الأجل في البيع المعين والثمن المعين (الخ قال الرملي أقول في جواهر الفتاوى : رجل له على آخر حنطة غير السلم فباعها منه بثمن معلوم إلى شهر لا يجوز لأن هذا بيع الكاليء بالكاليء وقد نهينا عنه وإن باعها ممن عليه ونقد المشتري الثمن في المجلس جاز فيكون ديناً بعين اهـ. وقد ذكر المسألة في منح الغفار في باب القرض قبل باب الربا نقلاً عن البزازية، وسيأتي في شرح قوله ويباع الطعام كيلاً وجزافاً» نقلاً عن البزازية : له عليه حنطة أكلها فباعها منه نسيئة لا يجوز. أقول : ومثله الزيت وكل مكيل وموزون ومثل البيع الصلح قال في الفصل الثلاثين من جامع الفصولين: ولو غصب كربر فصالحه وهو قائم على دراهم مؤجلة جاز، وكذا الذهب والفضة وسائر الموزونات، ولو صالحه على كيل مؤجل لم يجز إذ الجنس بانفراده يحرم النساء، ولو كان البر هالكاً لم يجز الصلح على شيء من هذا نسيئة لأنه دين بدين إلا إذا صالح على بر مثله أو أقل منه مؤجلاً جاز لأنه عين حقه والحط جائز لا لو على أكثر للربا والصلح على بعض حقه في الكيلي والوزني حال قيامه لم يجز اهـ. وذكر في البزارية الحيلة في جواز بيع الجنطة المستهلكة بالنسيئة أنه يبيعها بثوب ويقبض الثوب ثم يبيعه بدراهم إلى أجل أقول وتجري هذه الحيلة في الصلح أيضاً
As for the conditions of validity, they are general and specific. The general ones are each that is a condition for proper formation, because that which does not form a sale is not valid, and it does not reverse. Indeed, the invalid (fasid) in our view is formed and enforceable if possession is connected. Among them: it must not be temporary, for if he times it, it is not valid, unlike rental because temporariness is its condition. Among them: the sold item and price must be known with knowledge that prevents dispute. What is unknown with ignorance leading to dispute is not valid, like the flock, and selling a thing at its value or as so-and-so judges. Among them: freedom from a corrupting condition, and it is of types: a condition that creates uncertainty (gharar) like stipulating the pregnancy of an animal; the narrations differ on stipulating the pregnancy of a slave girl, and some favor that if the seller stipulates it, it is valid and a waiver, but if the buyer stipulates to take her as a wet nurse, it is invalid. Among them: if one buys a ram on condition it is butting. Among them: a condition not required by the contract that benefits one party, details to come. Among them: stipulating a time limit in a specific sold item and specific price, which is only permissible for debt. Among them: stipulating perpetual option. Among them: stipulating an option for an unknown period. Among them: stipulating absolute option like a sheep; the correct is opposite it. His saying: (and the ward being minor) refutes the restriction to the insane. His saying: (Second, that there be no right of another) ... (intervening gloss) ... Among them: stipulating an option for a known period exceeding three days. Among them: excepting the pregnancy of a slave girl. Among them: consent, so the sale of the coerced and his purchase are invalid, likewise the sale of talji'ah, and the first [contract] is owned by possession, not the second. Among them: benefit: selling that which has no benefit and buying it is invalid, so the sale of a dirham for a dirham equal in weight and quality is invalid; as in al-Dhakhira. As for the specific conditions, among them: the term must be known in a sale with deferred price; if unknown, it is invalid. Among them: possession in the buyer's sale of movable property and debt; selling debt before taking possession is invalid like salam capital, even after cancellation. Selling something for the debt owed by so-and-so, unlike if the debt is owed by the seller. Among them: the countervalue must be named in one of the types of exchange, which is verbal; if silent, it is invalid but ownership occurs by possession; if he negates it, some say invalid, some say void so no ownership by possession. In al-Tatimma: he sold it for a debt owed by him and they both knew he owes it, it is not valid. Among them: equality of the countervalues in ribawi goods, details to come. Among them: freedom from suspicion of riba. Among them: existence of the conditions of salam to come. Among them: possession in currency exchange before separation. Among them: the first price must be known in sales of murabahah, tawliyah, ishrak, and wadi'ah. As for the conditions of bindingness after formation and enforceability: freedom from the four well-known options, and added are the option of quantity, option of deception if there is deceit, option of entitlement of part of the sold item for a specific good absolutely and for fungible before possession, option of betrayal in murabahah, option of paying the price or not, option of revealing the situation, option of losing a desired attribute, option of ratification of a sale by a mediator, option of destruction of part of the sold item; these are three: ... [intervening gloss] ... His saying: (And from it: stipulating a term in a specific sold item and specific price) – al-Ramli said: I say in Jawahir al-Fatawa: A man owes another wheat not as salam, he sells it to him for a known price payable in a month – not permissible because this is selling debt for debt, which is prohibited. If he sells it to the debtor and the buyer pays the price in the session, it is permissible, becoming debt for an item. Mentioned in Manh al-Ghaffar... and in al-Bazzaziyyah the legal trick to permit selling consumed wheat on credit: he sells it for a garment, takes possession of the garment, then sells it for dirhams on credit. I say this trick also applies to sulh.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage is an extensive ruling statement enumerating the conditions of validity for a sale. The author divides conditions into general (common to all sales) and specific (pertaining to particular types). General conditions include: (1) The sale must not be temporary (unlike rental which requires a term). (2) Both the object and price must be known sufficiently to prevent dispute; unknown things leading to dispute are invalid. (3) Freedom from invalidating conditions, which include: stipulations causing gharar (uncertainty) like pregnancy of an animal (with differing rulings on a slave girl), stipulations not required by the contract that benefit one party, stipulating a time in a specific object (only valid for debts), perpetual option, unknown option period, etc. (4) Consent: the sale of a coerced person is invalid. (5) Benefit: selling something devoid of benefit (like a dirham for a dirham equal in weight) is invalid. Specific conditions for particular sales: (1) In deferred payment sales, the term must be known. (2) In sales involving movable property or debt, possession before sale is required; selling debt before taking possession is invalid. (3) In sales of ribawi items, equality of countervalues is required. (4) In currency exchange (sarf), possession before separation. (5) In murabahah, tawliyah, etc., the first price must be known. The passage also touches on conditions of bindingness (luzum), which include absence of major options and additional options. The gloss at the end addresses the prohibition of selling debt for debt (بيع الكاليء بالكاليء) and provides a legal trick (hila) to avoid it: selling the wheat for a garment, taking possession, then selling the garment on credit.

Key Terms
البيع الفاسد (al-bay' al-fasid): A sale that is valid in formation but invalid in description; it becomes enforceable if possession is taken.
شرط مفسد (shart mufsid): A condition that invalidates the sale, such as gharar or a condition not required by the contract benefiting one party.
بيع الكاليء بالكاليء (bay' al-kali' bi al-kali'): Sale of debt for debt, which is prohibited.
الحيلة (al-hilah): A legal stratagem to achieve a permissible outcome while avoiding a prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a general condition of validity for a sale?
A) The sale must be temporary.
B) The object and price must be known sufficiently to prevent dispute.
C) The sale must involve an option period.
D) The price must be paid in full immediately.

Key Takeaway
A valid sale in the Hanafi school requires that it be immediate, well-known, free of invalidating conditions, consensual, and beneficial.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Specific Conditions of Validity and Conditions of Bindingness
Listing of specific conditions and conditions of bindingness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the specific conditions for deferred sales, debt sales, ribawi items, and currency exchange, and identify the conditions of bindingness (luzum) that ensure a sale is irrevocable.

Core Idea
Specific conditions adjust validity for particular sales; bindingness requires freedom from options and hidden defects.

This passage continues from the general conditions to outline the specific conditions that apply to certain types of sales, such as those with deferred payments, sales involving debt, ribawi goods, and currency exchange (sarf). It also introduces the conditions of bindingness (luzum), which must be present for a sale to become irrevocable. A legal trick (hila) to avoid the prohibition of selling debt for debt is also provided.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first specific condition: known term in deferred payment sales
If the price is deferred, the term must be known; otherwise the sale is invalid (fasid).
2
Identify the second specific condition: possession required before selling movable or debt
Selling debt before taking possession is invalid; this applies to salam capital and even after cancellation.
3
Identify the third specific condition: naming the countervalue in verbal exchange
In verbal contracts (like barter), the countervalue must be specified; silence or negation may invalidate.
4
Identify the fourth specific condition: equality in ribawi items and freedom from riba suspicion
In transactions involving ribawi goods, equality of weight/measure is required; also, transactions must be free from any suspicion of riba.
5
Identify the conditions of bindingness and the prohibition of selling debt for debt
Bindingness requires absence of major options and additional ones. The passage ends with a discussion of bay' al-kali' bi al-kali' and a hila to avoid it.

والوضيعة. وأما شرائط اللزوم بعد الانعقاد والنفاذ فخلوه من الخيارات الأربعة المشهورة، ويزاد خيار الكمية وخيار الغبن إذا كان فيه غرور، وخيار استحقاق بعض المبيع القيمي مطلقاً والمثلي قبل القبض، وخيار الخيانة في المرابحة وخيار نقد الثمن ،وعدمه وخيار كشف الحال، وخيار فوات وصف مرغوب فيه، وخيار إجازة بيع الفضولي وخيار هلاك بعض المبيع فهي ثلاثة يصح لم ينعقد لأن ما لا يصح منه منعقد كالفاسد ومنه غيره كالباطل وفي قوله «منعقد نافذ» نظر فإن بيع المكره من الفاسد كما قدمه وهو منعقد موقوف وكان الظاهر أن يقول منعقد مملوك تأمل . قوله : (ومنه شرط الأجل في البيع المعين والثمن المعين (الخ قال الرملي أقول في جواهر الفتاوى : رجل له على آخر حنطة غير السلم فباعها منه بثمن معلوم إلى شهر لا يجوز لأن هذا بيع الكاليء بالكاليء وقد نهينا عنه وإن باعها ممن عليه ونقد المشتري الثمن في المجلس جاز فيكون ديناً بعين اهـ. وقد ذكر المسألة في منح الغفار في باب القرض قبل باب الربا نقلاً عن البزازية، وسيأتي في شرح قوله ويباع الطعام كيلاً وجزافاً» نقلاً عن البزازية : له عليه حنطة أكلها فباعها منه نسيئة لا يجوز. أقول : ومثله الزيت وكل مكيل وموزون ومثل البيع الصلح قال في الفصل الثلاثين من جامع الفصولين: ولو غصب كربر فصالحه وهو قائم على دراهم مؤجلة جاز، وكذا الذهب والفضة وسائر الموزونات، ولو صالحه على كيل مؤجل لم يجز إذ الجنس بانفراده يحرم النساء، ولو كان البر هالكاً لم يجز الصلح على شيء من هذا نسيئة لأنه دين بدين إلا إذا صالح على بر مثله أو أقل منه مؤجلاً جاز لأنه عين حقه والحط جائز لا لو على أكثر للربا والصلح على بعض حقه في الكيلي والوزني حال قيامه لم يجز اهـ. وذكر في البزارية الحيلة في جواز بيع الجنطة المستهلكة بالنسيئة أنه يبيعها بثوب ويقبض الثوب ثم يبيعه بدراهم إلى أجل أقول وتجري هذه الحيلة في الصلح أيضاً
As for the conditions of validity, they are general and specific. The general ones are each that is a condition for proper formation, because that which does not form a sale is not valid, and it does not reverse. Indeed, the invalid (fasid) in our view is formed and enforceable if possession is connected. Among them: it must not be temporary, for if he times it, it is not valid, unlike rental because temporariness is its condition. Among them: the sold item and price must be known with knowledge that prevents dispute. What is unknown with ignorance leading to dispute is not valid, like the flock, and selling a thing at its value or as so-and-so judges. Among them: freedom from a corrupting condition, and it is of types: a condition that creates uncertainty (gharar) like stipulating the pregnancy of an animal; the narrations differ on stipulating the pregnancy of a slave girl, and some favor that if the seller stipulates it, it is valid and a waiver, but if the buyer stipulates to take her as a wet nurse, it is invalid. Among them: if one buys a ram on condition it is butting. Among them: a condition not required by the contract that benefits one party, details to come. Among them: stipulating a time limit in a specific sold item and specific price, which is only permissible for debt. Among them: stipulating perpetual option. Among them: stipulating an option for an unknown period. Among them: stipulating absolute option like a sheep; the correct is opposite it. His saying: (and the ward being minor) refutes the restriction to the insane. His saying: (Second, that there be no right of another) ... (intervening gloss) ... Among them: stipulating an option for a known period exceeding three days. Among them: excepting the pregnancy of a slave girl. Among them: consent, so the sale of the coerced and his purchase are invalid, likewise the sale of talji'ah, and the first [contract] is owned by possession, not the second. Among them: benefit: selling that which has no benefit and buying it is invalid, so the sale of a dirham for a dirham equal in weight and quality is invalid; as in al-Dhakhira. As for the specific conditions, among them: the term must be known in a sale with deferred price; if unknown, it is invalid. Among them: possession in the buyer's sale of movable property and debt; selling debt before taking possession is invalid like salam capital, even after cancellation. Selling something for the debt owed by so-and-so, unlike if the debt is owed by the seller. Among them: the countervalue must be named in one of the types of exchange, which is verbal; if silent, it is invalid but ownership occurs by possession; if he negates it, some say invalid, some say void so no ownership by possession. In al-Tatimma: he sold it for a debt owed by him and they both knew he owes it, it is not valid. Among them: equality of the countervalues in ribawi goods, details to come. Among them: freedom from suspicion of riba. Among them: existence of the conditions of salam to come. Among them: possession in currency exchange before separation. Among them: the first price must be known in sales of murabahah, tawliyah, ishrak, and wadi'ah. As for the conditions of bindingness after formation and enforceability: freedom from the four well-known options, and added are the option of quantity, option of deception if there is deceit, option of entitlement of part of the sold item for a specific good absolutely and for fungible before possession, option of betrayal in murabahah, option of paying the price or not, option of revealing the situation, option of losing a desired attribute, option of ratification of a sale by a mediator, option of destruction of part of the sold item; these are three: ... [intervening gloss] ... His saying: (And from it: stipulating a term in a specific sold item and specific price) – al-Ramli said: I say in Jawahir al-Fatawa: A man owes another wheat not as salam, he sells it to him for a known price payable in a month – not permissible because this is selling debt for debt, which is prohibited. If he sells it to the debtor and the buyer pays the price in the session, it is permissible, becoming debt for an item. Mentioned in Manh al-Ghaffar... and in al-Bazzaziyyah the legal trick to permit selling consumed wheat on credit: he sells it for a garment, takes possession of the garment, then sells it for dirhams on credit. I say this trick also applies to sulh.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage is an extensive ruling statement enumerating the conditions of validity for a sale. The author divides conditions into general (common to all sales) and specific (pertaining to particular types). General conditions include: (1) The sale must not be temporary (unlike rental which requires a term). (2) Both the object and price must be known sufficiently to prevent dispute; unknown things leading to dispute are invalid. (3) Freedom from invalidating conditions, which include: stipulations causing gharar (uncertainty) like pregnancy of an animal (with differing rulings on a slave girl), stipulations not required by the contract that benefit one party, stipulating a time in a specific object (only valid for debts), perpetual option, unknown option period, etc. (4) Consent: the sale of a coerced person is invalid. (5) Benefit: selling something devoid of benefit (like a dirham for a dirham equal in weight) is invalid. Specific conditions for particular sales: (1) In deferred payment sales, the term must be known. (2) In sales involving movable property or debt, possession before sale is required; selling debt before taking possession is invalid. (3) In sales of ribawi items, equality of countervalues is required. (4) In currency exchange (sarf), possession before separation. (5) In murabahah, tawliyah, etc., the first price must be known. The passage also touches on conditions of bindingness (luzum), which include absence of major options and additional options. The gloss at the end addresses the prohibition of selling debt for debt (بيع الكاليء بالكاليء) and provides a legal trick (hila) to avoid it: selling the wheat for a garment, taking possession, then selling the garment on credit.

Key Terms
البيع الفاسد (al-bay' al-fasid): A sale that is valid in formation but invalid in description; it becomes enforceable if possession is taken.
شرط مفسد (shart mufsid): A condition that invalidates the sale, such as gharar or a condition not required by the contract benefiting one party.
بيع الكاليء بالكاليء (bay' al-kali' bi al-kali'): Sale of debt for debt, which is prohibited.
الحيلة (al-hilah): A legal stratagem to achieve a permissible outcome while avoiding a prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on selling a debt (like salam capital) before taking possession?
A) Valid, because the debt is known.
B) Invalid (fasid).
C) Valid only if the buyer is the debtor.
D) Void (batil) from the outset.

Key Takeaway
Specific conditions tailor the validity of particular sales; bindingness requires freedom from options, and selling debt for debt is strictly prohibited, though legal tricks exist.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
beginner
8 min
General Conditions and the Fāsid/Bāṭil Distinction
Listing general conditions and effects on validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general conditions of a valid sale and distinguish between a fāsid and a bāṭil sale according to the Hanafī school.

Core Idea
A sale is fāsid if it fails a condition of validity but is concluded; a bāṭil sale is void from the start.

This lesson introduces the essential prerequisites for a sale to be legally concluded (munʿaqid) and the consequences of missing certain conditions. The Hanafī school uniquely categorizes corrupt sales (fāsid) as a middle ground between valid and void, where ownership transfers only upon possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three general conditions mentioned
From the text: (1) a temporary known option beyond three days, (2) exclusion of the slave-girl's pregnancy, (3) mutual consent.
These are conditions that can be stipulated or are inherent.
2
Understand the effect of coercion
The sale of a coerced person (mukrah) is fāsid – it is concluded (munʿaqid) but ownership transfers only upon possession (qabḍ).
The text explicitly says 'ففسد المكره وشراؤه'.
3
Distinguish fāsid from bāṭil using the example of a dirham-for-dirham sale
Selling a dirham for a dirham equal in weight and quality is fāsid because it has no benefit; it is not bāṭil. A fāsid sale transfers ownership upon possession, while a bāṭil sale does not.
The text says 'بيع ما لا فائدة فيه وشراؤه فاسد' and then 'فسد بيع درهم بدرهم'.

Source Text
