Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq: 'Sale is binding by offer and acceptance.' It begins with a brief historical context about the Prophet's own trading before prophethood and the trade of the Companions, establishing the virtue of trade. The core of the passage is a detailed legal discussion on the definition of sale (bay'). The author examines two main views: (1) Sale is merely the ruling (hukm) of ownership that results from offer and acceptance, as posited by some scholars including the author of Kanz. (2) Sale is a composite of offer, acceptance, and the legal link (irtibat) between them, which is a third element. The author sides with the second view, citing authorities like al-`Inayah and al-Binayah. He argues that if sale were only the ruling, then the phrase 'sale is concluded by offer and acceptance' would be problematic because it would mean 'the ruling is concluded by the cause', which is circular. Instead, the correct understanding is that the legal contract (al-`aqd al-shar`i) is a separate entity established by offer and acceptance, and sale is the name for the combination of these three: offer, acceptance, and the legal link. The passage also provides linguistic definitions of 'ijab' and 'qabul' and discusses cases where actions replace verbal acceptance, such as taking possession. The author concludes by clarifying that the restriction of bindingness (luzum) by offer and acceptance means that once both are exchanged, no further action (like the seller's permission) is needed. This lesson is essential for understanding the fundamental nature of contracts in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
البيع (al-bay'): A legal contract (عقد) that transfers ownership of a commodity for a price, consisting of offer, acceptance, and the legal link between them.
الإيجاب (al-ijāb): The first utterance from either contracting party indicating consent, such as 'I sold' or 'I bought'.
القبول (al-qabūl): The second utterance (or action) that responds to and accepts the offer, such as 'I accept' or taking possession with intent.
التعاطي (al-mu'ātah): A sale conducted through mutual giving without any verbal offer and acceptance, relying solely on actions.
الارتباط (al-irtibāṭ): The legal bond that links offer and acceptance, making them a single contractual entity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is considered a valid acceptance according to the Hanafi school?
A) Remaining silent after an offer
B) Taking possession after the seller says 'I sold it to you'
C) Saying 'I will think about it'
D) Leaving the session without responding

Key Takeaway
Acceptance in a sale can be verbal or through an appropriate action with intent, as long as it occurs in the same session and indicates consent.

The Ruling on Seller's Re-Approval and the Option of Acceptance
Explains a rare dispute on seller's re-approval and the option of acceptance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether the seller needs to re-approve after the buyer's acceptance and explain the offeror's right to withdraw before acceptance.

Core Idea
The contract is concluded by offer and matching acceptance; no subsequent approval is required by the majority of Hanafi scholars.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a rare scenario in which some early scholars thought a seller's re-approval was needed after the buyer's acceptance. The author clarifies the majority position and extracts general principles about the offeror's right to withdraw and the duration of the option of acceptance. Understanding this helps you grasp the fundamentals of contract formation in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rare scenario
The seller says 'I sold you this slave for such amount' and the buyer says 'I accept'. The question is whether the seller must then say 'I have agreed' for the contract to be valid.
2
State the rare view and its reasoning
Some mashayikh held that the seller must re-approve because when the seller says 'I sold you', ownership transfers to the buyer, so when the buyer says 'I bought', the buyer owns the slave and owes the price; thus the seller needs to re-express consent to own the price.
This view is not the adopted position.
3
Identify the majority view and its support
The majority of mashayikh hold no re-approval is needed; this is the correct view, reported from Imam Muhammad. The contract is concluded by the initial offer and acceptance.
This is the mufta bihi (operative ruling).
4
Extract the general rule on offeror withdrawal
The offeror may withdraw before acceptance because he has not nullified anyone's right. The withdrawal must be heard by the other party, but according to one view, it is valid even if not known.
5
Understand the option of acceptance and its duration
The option of acceptance (khiyar al-qabul) extends until the end of the session, which is treated as a single unit to achieve ease.
The session ends when the parties separate or when one leaves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن من قال لغيره بعت منك هذا العبد بكذا فقال المشتري قبلت أن البيع لا ينعقد بينهما ما لم يقل البائع بعد ذلك أجزت، وبه قال بعض المشايخ. وهذا لأن البائع لما قال بعت منك فقد ملك العبد من المشتري فإذا قال المشتري اشتريت فقد تملك العبد وملكه الثمن فلا بد من إجازة البائع بعد ذلك ليملك الثمن، وعامة المشايخ على أنه لا يحتاج إلى إجازة البائع بعد ذلك اهـ. وهو الصحيح وهكذا روي عن محمد اهـ. وينبغي حفظه لغرابته ولأنه إذا أوجب أحدهما فللآخر أن لا يقبل لأنه لا يلزمه حكم العقد بدون رضاه وللموجب أن يرجع لخلوه عن إبطال حق الغير لأن الموجب أثبت له حق أن يتملك مع ثبوت حقيقة الملك له والحقيقة مقدمة على الحق، ولا بد من سماع الآخر رجوع الموجب كما في التتارخانية. وفي التتمة : يصح الرجوع وإن لم يعلم به الآخر وإنما يمتد خيار القبول إلى آخر المجلس لكونه جامعاً للمتفرقات فاعتبرت ساعاته ساعة واحدة دفعاً للعسر وتحقيقاً لليسر وسيأتي بيان ما يبطله .
That if someone said to another: 'I sold you this slave for such amount,' and the buyer said 'I accept,' the sale is not concluded between them unless the seller after that says 'I have agreed.' Some mashayikh held this view. This is because when the seller said 'I sold you,' he transferred ownership of the slave to the buyer, so when the buyer says 'I bought,' he acquires ownership of the slave and owes the price; therefore the seller's subsequent approval is needed for him to own the price. But the majority of mashayikh hold that he does not need the seller's subsequent approval. That is the correct view, and it is transmitted from Muhammad. It should be memorized due to its rarity. And because when one of them makes an offer, the other may not accept, since the ruling of the contract does not become binding without his consent, and the offeror may withdraw because he has not nullified the right of another, since the offeror has established for himself a right to take ownership, while the reality of ownership is established for him, and reality takes precedence over right. The offeror's withdrawal must be heard by the other, as in al-Tatarkhaniyyah. And in al-Tatimmah: the withdrawal is valid even if the other does not know of it. And the option of acceptance extends until the end of the session, because the session brings together scattered moments, so its hours are considered as one hour, to remove hardship and achieve ease. And later will come the explanation of what nullifies it.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses a specific scenario in the contract of sale. The author presents a rare view: if the seller says 'I sold you this slave for such amount' and the buyer responds 'I accept,' some early scholars said the contract is not concluded until the seller says 'I have agreed' again. Their reasoning is that the seller's original statement transfers ownership to the buyer, so when the buyer says 'I bought,' he owns the slave and owes the price; thus the seller needs to re-express his consent to own the price. However, the majority of scholars (عامة المشايخ) hold that no such re-approval is needed, and this is the correct view, reported from Imam Muhammad. The author advises memorizing this due to its rarity. The passage then explains the general rule: when one party makes an offer, the other may reject it because the contract is not binding without his consent. Also, the offeror may withdraw his offer before acceptance, since he has not nullified anyone's right. This is because the offeror has established a right for himself to take ownership, but the reality of ownership is already established for the offeree (since the offeror's statement of sale transfers ownership in theory), and reality takes precedence over right. The withdrawal must be heard by the other party (i.e., communicated). According to al-Tatimmah, the withdrawal is valid even if the other does not know of it. The option of acceptance (خيار القبول) extends until the end of the session, because the session is considered as a single unit to facilitate ease, following the principle that all parts of the session are treated as one time. The author notes that what nullifies this option will be explained later.

Key Terms
الإيجاب (al-ijab): The offer made by the seller in a sale contract, e.g., 'I sold you this slave for such amount.'
القبول (al-qabul): The acceptance by the buyer, e.g., 'I accept' or 'I bought.'
خيار القبول (khiyar al-qabul): The option of acceptance: the offeree's right to accept or reject the offer within the session.
المجلس (al-majlis): The session of contracting, considered as a single unit from the start of the offer to the end of the gathering.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Majority of Mashayikh, after the seller says 'I sold you this slave for such amount' and the buyer says 'I accept', is the seller's re-approval (e.g., 'I have agreed') needed for the contract to be valid?
A) Yes, the seller must say 'I have agreed' again.
B) No, the contract is concluded without further approval.
C) It depends on whether the slave is present.
D) Only if the buyer does not pay immediately.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi majority holds that a sale contract is concluded by the initial offer and acceptance; no further approval from the seller is necessary.

Khiyar al-Majlis: The Shafi'i Proof and Hanafi Refutation
Presents the Shafi'i proof for khiyar al-majlis and the Hanafi refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi interpretation of the hadith 'البيعان بالخيار ما لم يتفرقا' and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school denies khiyar al-majlis by interpreting the hadith as referring to pre-contract negotiation.

This lesson examines one of the most well-known inter-madhhab disputes in sales law: the option of the session. The Shafi'i school relies on the apparent meaning of a sahih hadith to grant both parties the right to rescind after the contract. The Hanafi school, however, interprets the same hadith figuratively, arguing that the option applies only before the contract is finalized. Understanding this dispute reveals how different usul principles (literal vs. figurative interpretation, the authority of narrator understanding) generate divergent rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core hadith and its apparent meaning
The hadith: 'البيعان بالخيار ما لم يتفرقا' (The two buyers have the option as long as they have not separated). Shafi'is take this literally as an option after the contract.
2
State the Hanafi interpretation of key terms
Hanafis interpret 'al-bayyi'ani' as 'those negotiating' (mutasawiman), not actual buyers after contract. 'Tafarruq' is interpreted either as separation of bodies before acceptance (Abu Yusuf) or separation of statements (Muhammad).
3
Understand the Hanafi argument from necessity of metaphor
If literal meaning is impossible (because 'buyers' before contract is a metaphor), then a metaphor is necessary. Between two metaphors, the closer one to reality is chosen.
4
Analyze the second version of the hadith
The variant saying 'If they separate after transacting, the sale is binding' supports the Hanafi view that the option is only before the contract.
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In Hanafi fiqh, a sale is binding immediately; no rescission for session. In Shafi'i, both parties may rescind until physical separation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار باللزوم بهما إلى أنهما لو أقرا ببيع ولم يكن بينهما حقيقة لم ينعقد كما في الصيرفية، وإلى نفي خيار المجلس عندنا، ولولا هذه الإشارة لكان التعبير بالانعقاد تبعاً للقوم أولى لأن المترتب عليهما إنما هو الانعقاد، وأما اللزوم فموقوف على شرائط أخر مخصوصة كما في إيضاح الإصلاح. وأثبته الشافعي عملاً بحديث البخاري عن ابن عمر رضي الله عنهما مرفوعاً «البيعان بالخيار ما لم يتفرقا » (۱) وأوله أبو يوسف بتفرق الأبدان بعد الإيجاب قبل القبول، وأوله محمد تبعاً لإبراهيم النخعي بتفرق الأقوال بناء على أن المراد بالخيار فيه خيار القبول، واعتمده في الهداية بأن في الحديث إشارة إليه فإنهما متبايعان حالة المباشرة لا بعدها، ويؤيده قوله تعالى وإن يتفرقا يغن الله كلا من سعته ﴾ [النساء: ١٣١] فإن الفرقة تحصل بقولهما وإن داما جالسين وهو مبني على أن اسم الفاعل حقيقة في الحال وفيه نظر، لأن تسميتهما متبايعين قبل تمام العقد مجاز آخر، وإذا تعذر الحمل على الحقيقة تعين المجاز، وإذا تعارض المجازان فالأقرب إلى الحقيقة أولى؛ كذا في فتح الباري. وقال البيضاوي : ومن نفی خيار المجلس ارتكب مجازين؛ حمله التفرق على الأقوال وحمله المتبايعين على المتساومين . وأيضاً فكلام الشارع يصان عن الحمل عليه لأنه يصير التقدير أن المتساومين إن شاءا عقدا وإن شاءا لم يعقدا وهو تحصيل الحاصل اهـ. وقد استدل في البناية بقوله تعالى أوفوا بالعقود [المائدة: ١] والبيع عقد فيجب الوفاء به، وبقوله تعالى واشهدوا إذا تبايعتم [البقرة: ٢٨٢] أمر بالإشهاد للتوثق ، فلو كان له الخيار لم يكن له معنى. وبقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام لحبان بن منقذ «إذا بايعت فقل لا خلابة (۱) ولو كان له خيار لم يحتج إليه اهـ. وفيه نظر لجواز أن يكون الكل بعد الافتراق لا قبله. ورجح عيسى بن أبان الأول بأن المعهود في الشرع أن الفرقة بالبدن موجبة للفساد كما في الصرف حال القبض. واختلف المتأخرون في معنى التفرق بالأقوال ففي المستصفى وفتح القدير : وهو أن يقول الآخر بعد الإيجاب لا أقبل فالتفرق رد القول الأول كتفرق بني إسرائيل اثنين وسبعين فرقة بمعنى اختلاف عقائدهم . وفي غاية البيان : هو قبول الآخر بعد الإيجاب فإذا قبله فقد تفرقا وانقطع الخيار كتفرق الزوجين. فعلى الأول إذا وجد التفرق لم يبق البيع أصلاً، وعلى الثاني لم يبق الخيار ولزم البيع، وقد فهم الراوي - أعني ابن عمر رضي الله عنهما - خيار المجلس من الحديث فكان كما رواه البخاري إذا اشترى شيئاً يعجبه فارق صاحبه لكن تأويل الراوي لا يكون حجة عندنا على غيره. وفي فتح الباري عن ابن خرم أن خيار المجلس ثابت بهذا الحديث، سواء قلنا التفرق بالكلام أو بالأبدان. فإن قلنا بالأبدان ،فواضح، وكذا إن قلنا بالأقوال لأن قول أحدهما بعتكه بعشرة وقول الآخر لا بل بعشرين افتراق في الكلام بخلاف ما لو قال اشتريته بعشرة فإنهما متوافقان فيتعين ثبوت الخيار لهما. فعلى هذا إذا وجد التفرق انقطع البيع لا أنه ينقطع الخيار، وظاهر الحديث انقطاع الخيار به مع بقاء العقد، وإذا احتمل فلم يبق حجة على معين وقد روى البخاري رواية أخرى عن ابن عمر مرفوعاً إذا تبايع الرجلان فكل واحد منهما بالخيار ما لم يتفرقا أو يخير أحدهما الآخر وكانا جميعاً، وإن تفرقا بعد أن تبايعا ولم يترك فقد الوجب وجب البيع»(۲) وهو ظاهر في انفساخ البيع بفسخ أحدهما. قال الخطابي رحمه الله تعالى : هو أوضح شيء في ثبوت خيار المجلس مبطل لكل تأويل مخالف لظاهر الحديث. وكذلك قوله في آخره وإن تفرقا بعد أن تبايعا فيه البيان الواضح على أن التفرق بالأبدان، ولو كان معناه بالقول لخلا الحديث عن الفائدة؛ كذا في فتح الباري.
And he indicated by 'binding through both' that if they both confess to a sale without it actually occurring, it is not concluded, as in al-Sayrafiyyah, and that the option of the session is negated in our school. Were it not for this indication, the expression of conclusion would have been more appropriate following the people, because what results from them is only conclusion. As for binding, it depends on specific other conditions, as in Idah al-Islah. Al-Shafi'i affirmed it acting upon the hadith of Bukhari from Ibn Umar, marfu': 'The two buyers have the option as long as they have not separated.' Abu Yusuf interpreted it as separation of bodies after offer and before acceptance. Muhammad followed Ibrahim al-Nakha'i in interpreting it as separation of statements, built on the view that the 'option' here means the option of acceptance. This was relied upon in al-Hidayah because the hadith contains an indication to it: they are both buyers during the direct contracting, not after. This is supported by Allah's statement: 'And if they separate, Allah will enrich each from His abundance' [4:131], for separation occurs by their statements even if they remain seated. This is based on the participle being a reality for the present tense, but there is consideration, because calling them buyers before the completion of the contract is another metaphor. When literal meaning is impossible, metaphor becomes necessary. When two metaphors conflict, the one closer to reality is preferable. So in Fath al-Bari. Al-Baydawi said: He who negates the option of the session employs two metaphors: interpreting 'separation' as statements and interpreting 'the two buyers' as negotiators. Also, the words of the Legislator should be protected from such interpretation, because it would render the meaning: 'the negotiators may contract if they wish or not contract if they wish,' which is a tautology. Al-Binayah adduced the verse 'Fulfill the contracts' [5:1] – the sale is a contract so it must be fulfilled, and the verse 'And call witnesses when you transact [2:282] commands witnessing for documentation; if there were an option, it would be meaningless. And the Prophet's saying to Hibban ibn Munqidh: 'When you buy, say: no deception' – if he had an option, he would not need this. There is consideration: it is possible that all this is after separation, not before. Isa ibn Aban gave preponderance to the first view because the established norm in the Shari'ah is that separation by body causes invalidity, as in the case of sarf at the time of receipt. The later scholars differed on the meaning of 'separation by statements.' In al-Mustasfa and Fath al-Qadir: it is that the other says after the offer 'I do not accept,' so the separation is rejection of the first statement, like the division of the Children of Israel into 72 sects, meaning their differing beliefs. In Ghayat al-Bayan: it is the other's acceptance after the offer; when he accepts, they separate and the option is cut off, like the separation of spouses. According to the first, when separation occurs, the sale does not remain at all; according to the second, the option does not remain and the sale becomes binding. The narrator, Ibn Umar himself, understood the option of the session from the hadith, so he would, as Bukhari narrated, if he bought something he liked, he would leave his companion. But the interpretation of a narrator is not proof for others in our school. In Fath al-Bari from Ibn Khurm: the option of the session is established by this hadith, whether we say separation is by words or bodies. If by bodies, it is obvious; likewise if by words, because one saying 'I sell it to you for ten' and the other saying 'No, for twenty' is separation in words, unlike if one said 'I buy it for ten' then they are in agreement. According to this, when separation occurs, the sale is cut off, not that the option is cut off. The apparent meaning of the hadith is that the option is cut off while the contract remains. Since it is probable, it is not a definitive proof for a specific meaning. Bukhari narrated another version from Ibn Umar, marfu': 'When two men transact, each of them has the option as long as they have not separated or one gives the other the option and they are both together. And if they separate after transacting and neither has rescinded, the sale becomes binding.' This is apparent in the dissolution of the sale by rescission of one of them. Al-Khattabi said: It is the clearest thing in establishing the option of the session, invalidating any interpretation contrary to the apparent meaning. Likewise his saying at the end: 'And if they separate after transacting' contains clear evidence that separation is by bodies; if it meant by words, the hadith would be devoid of benefit. So in Fath al-Bari.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage presents the inter-madhhab dispute on khiyar al-majlis (option of the session). The Hanafi school denies this option, while the Shafi'i school affirms it based on the hadith of Ibn Umar: 'The two buyers have the option as long as they have not separated.' The Hanafi response involves interpreting the hadith figuratively. Abu Yusuf interpreted 'separation' as separation of bodies (the parties physically parting) after the offer but before acceptance. Muhammad, following Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, interpreted it as separation of statements (i.e., the offer and acceptance being different or a rejection). The Hanafi argument is that the term 'al-bayyi'ani' (the two buyers) in the hadith refers to the parties before the contract is concluded, i.e., they are negotiators (mutasawiman), not actual buyers. So the option mentioned is the option of acceptance before the contract is finalized, not an option after concluding the contract. The text also cites the Quranic verse 'If they separate, Allah will enrich them' to support that separation can happen by words. Al-Baydawi criticized this interpretation as involving two metaphors, but the Hanafi response is that if literal interpretation is impossible, metaphor is necessary, and the closer metaphor should be chosen. Additional proofs from the Quran and hadith are presented by the Shafi'is, but the Hanafis respond that those are not necessarily applicable before the contract is concluded. The later scholars differed on the meaning of 'separation by statements': some say it is a rejection of the offer, others say it is the acceptance itself. The passage also discusses a second version of the hadith that seems to support the Hanafi view, as it mentions that if they separate after transacting (i.e., after the contract is done), the sale becomes binding, indicating that the option is only before the contract. Ultimately, the Hanafi school holds that there is no option of the session after the contract is concluded; the buyer and seller do not have the right to rescind the sale simply because they are still in the session.

Key Terms
خيار المجلس (khiyar al-majlis): The option of the session: the right of both buyer and seller to rescind the sale as long as they remain in the same physical location after the contract.
البيعان (al-bayyi'ani): The two buyers (dual form) – in the hadith, the two parties engaged in a sale transaction. Hanafis interpret this as negotiators before contract conclusion.
التفرق (al-tafarruq): Separation – in the hadith, the condition that ends the option. Hanafis interpret it as separation of statements (acceptance or rejection) or optionally as physical separation before acceptance.
المتساومان (al-mutasawiman): Two persons negotiating a price without having concluded a contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what does 'tafarruq' (separation) in the hadith 'البيعان بالخيار ما لم يتفرقا' primarily refer to?
A) Physical separation of the two parties
B) Separation of statements (e.g., acceptance or rejection)
C) The end of the session
D) The completion of payment

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rejects khiyar al-majlis by interpreting the hadith's key terms metaphorically, leading to immediate binding of the sale contract.

Verbal Formula for Offer and Acceptance: Past and Present Tense
States the ruling on using past or present tense in offer and acceptance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of offer and acceptance using past or present tense and explain when intention (niyyah) is required.

Core Idea
Past tense needs no intention; present tense requires intention to avoid future ambiguity.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the verbal formula for contracts. It explains that offer and acceptance are not restricted to the past tense, but the tense affects whether intention is needed. Understanding this nuance is crucial for valid contract formation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is offer and acceptance in a sale contract. The ruling is that it is valid (sahih) with any two expressions indicating transfer of ownership.
2
Distinguish past vs. present tense
When using past tense (e.g., 'I sold'), no intention is needed. When using present tense (e.g., 'I sell'), intention is required on the correct view.
Intention here means intending the immediate present, not the future.
3
Understand the reason for the difference
The present tense is literal for the present moment, but commonly used for the future. To avoid ambiguity, intention ensures the contract is immediate.
This is based on predominant usage (ghalabat al-isti'mal).
4
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
There is a difference of opinion on whether intention is needed with present tense; the author adopts the correct view (al-ashahh) that it is needed.
This is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأطلق في الإيجاب والقبول ولم يقيدهما بالماضي كما في الهداية لأن التحقيق أنه لا يتقيد بذلك لانعقاده بكل لفظين ينبئان عن معنى التملك والتمليك ماضيين أو حالين كما في الخانية لكن ينعقد بالماضي بلا نية، وبالمضارع بها على الأصح ؛ كذا في البدائع وإنما احتيج إليها مع كونه حقيقة للحال عندنا على الأصح لغلبة استعماله في الاستقبال حقيقة أو مجازاً؛ كذا في البدائع
And he made it absolute in offer and acceptance and did not restrict them to the past tense, as in al-Hidayah, because the reality is that it is not restricted to that, since the contract is concluded by any two expressions that indicate the meaning of taking ownership and giving ownership, whether past or present tense, as in al-Khaniyyah. However, it is concluded with the past tense without intention, and with the present tense with intention on the correct view. So in al-Bada'i. And intention is needed with the present tense despite it being literal for the present in our school on the correct view, due to the preponderance of its usage for the future really or metaphorically. So in al-Bada'i.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage clarifies the verbal formula (sigah) for offer and acceptance in a sale contract. The author states that the court (or the text) did not restrict the expressions to the past tense, unlike some other texts. The correct view is that offer and acceptance can be valid with any two expressions that convey the meaning of transfer of ownership (tamlik) and taking ownership (tamalluk), whether they are in the past tense (madi) or present tense (hal). However, there is a nuance: when using the past tense (e.g., 'I sold,' 'I bought'), the contract is concluded without the need for intention (niyyah) because the past tense is typically used for the past and is clear. When using the present tense (e.g., 'I sell,' 'I buy'), intention is required on the correct view (al-ashahh). This is because although the present tense is considered literal for the present moment (haqiqah li al-hal) in the Hanafi school, it is predominantly used for the future in common usage, either literally or metaphorically. Therefore, to ensure the parties intend an immediate contract, intention must be present. This ruling is found in al-Bada'i.

Key Terms
الماضي (al-madi): Past tense; e.g., 'birtu' (I sold) or 'ishtaraitu' (I bought).
المضارع (al-mudari'): Present tense; e.g., 'abi'u' (I sell) or 'ashtari' (I buy).
النية (al-niyyah): Intention to make the contract immediate.
حقيقة الحال (haqiqah li al-hal): Literal meaning for the present moment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on using the present tense for offer and acceptance according to the Hanafi school?
A) It is invalid in all cases.
B) It is valid only if the parties intend the future.
C) It is valid with intention (niyyah) on the correct view.
D) It is valid without any intention.

Key Takeaway
Past tense in offer and acceptance does not require intention; present tense requires intention to ensure an immediate contract.

The Option of the Session (Khiyar al-Majlis) and the Meaning of Tafarruq
Explaining hadith proof and dispute over tafarruq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on khiyar al-majlis, identify the hadith proof, and explain the dispute over the meaning of tafarruq.

Core Idea
The option of the session gives both parties the right to revoke until they physically separate—separation by speech is not sufficient.

In sales, the Prophet ﷺ established a right for both buyer and seller to change their minds as long as they remain in the same session. This right, known as khiyar al-majlis, is based on authentic hadith. However, scholars differed on what exactly ends this option: does it end when the parties physically part ways, or when they verbally disagree? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position and the evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Khiyar al-majlis is a confirmed sunnah for both parties in a sale. The ruling is that the option exists until the parties separate.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the hadith: 'When two men transact a sale, each of them has the option as long as they have not separated.' (Bukhari and Muslim).
Also the command 'la khilaba' to Hiban ibn Munqidh.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'ما لم يتفرقا' (as long as they have not separated) indicates that the option continues until separation occurs. The apparent meaning is physical departure.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some later Hanafis interpreted tafarruq as verbal disagreement (e.g., one party says 'I do not accept'), while the majority, including the author, take it as physical separation.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi, not between madhhabs.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is physical separation, supported by al-Khattabi's commentary and the need for a clear legal indicator.
The second hadith from Bukhari explicitly links separation to the termination of the option.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع [البقرة : ٢٨٢] أمر بالإشهاد للتوثق ، فلو كان له الخيار لم يكن له معنى. وبقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام لحبان بن منقذ «إذا بايعت فقل لا خلابة ولو كان له خيار لم يحتج إليه اهـ. وفيه نظر لجواز أن يكون الكل بعد الافتراق لا قبله. ورجح عيسى بن أبان الأول بأن المعهود في الشرع أن الفرقة بالبدن موجبة للفساد كما في الصرف حال القبض. واختلف المتأخرون في معنى التفرق بالأقوال ففي المستصفى وفتح القدير : وهو أن يقول الآخر بعد الإيجاب لا أقبل فالتفرق رد القول الأول كتفرق بني إسرائيل اثنين وسبعين فرقة بمعنى اختلاف عقائدهم . وفي غاية البيان : هو قبول الآخر بعد الإيجاب فإذا قبله فقد تفرقا وانقطع الخيار كتفرق الزوجين. فعلى الأول إذا وجد التفرق لم يبق البيع أصلاً، وعلى الثاني لم يبق الخيار ولزم البيع، وقد فهم الراوي - أعني ابن عمر رضي الله عنهما - خيار المجلس من الحديث فكان كما رواه البخاري إذا اشترى شيئاً يعجبه فارق صاحبه لكن تأويل الراوي لا يكون حجة عندنا على غيره. وفي فتح الباري عن ابن خرم أن خيار المجلس ثابت بهذا الحديث، سواء قلنا التفرق بالكلام أو بالأبدان. فإن قلنا بالأبدان ،فواضح، وكذا إن قلنا بالأقوال لأن قول أحدهما بعتكه بعشرة وقول الآخر لا بل بعشرين افتراق في الكلام بخلاف ما لو قال اشتريته بعشرة فإنهما متوافقان فيتعين ثبوت الخيار لهما. فعلى هذا إذا وجد التفرق انقطع البيع لا أنه ينقطع الخيار، وظاهر الحديث انقطاع الخيار به مع بقاء العقد، وإذا احتمل فلم يبق حجة على معين وقد روى البخاري رواية أخرى عن ابن عمر مرفوعاً إذا تبايع الرجلان فكل واحد منهما بالخيار ما لم يتفرقا أو يخير أحدهما الآخر وكانا جميعاً، وإن تفرقا بعد أن تبايعا ولم يترك فقد البيع وجب البيع» وهو ظاهر في انفساخ البيع بفسخ أحدهما. قال الخطابي رحمه الله تعالى : هو أوضح شيء في ثبوت خيار المجلس مبطل لكل تأويل مخالف لظاهر الحديث. وكذلك قوله في آخره وإن تفرقا بعد أن تبايعا فيه البيان الواضح على أن التفرق بالأبدان، ولو كان معناه بالقول لخلا الحديث عن الفائدة؛ كذا في فتح الباري.
The Book of Sale. [Quran 2:282] commanded witnessing for security, so if he had the option there would be no meaning for it. And by his statement, peace be upon him, to Hiban ibn Munqidh: 'When you buy, say: No deception' and if he had an option he would not have needed it. End. But there is consideration because it is possible that the entire [option] is after separation, not before. And Isa ibn Aban preferred the first [view] because what is customary in the law is that separation by the body causes nullification, as in currency exchange at the time of hand-to-hand receipt. The later scholars differed about the meaning of separation by statements: in al-Mustasfa and Fath al-Qadir: it is that the other says after the offer 'I do not accept', so separation is a rejection of the first statement, like the separation of the Children of Israel into seventy-two sects in the sense of differing beliefs. And in Ghayat al-Bayan: it is the acceptance of the other after the offer, so when he accepts it they have separated and the option is cut off, like the separation of spouses. According to the first, if separation occurs the sale does not remain at all, and according to the second the option does not remain and the sale is binding. And the narrator - meaning Ibn Umar, may Allah be pleased with them both - understood the option of the session from the hadith, and according to what al-Bukhari narrated: when he bought something he liked he would leave his companion. But the interpretation of the narrator is not a proof with us for others. And in Fath al-Bari from Ibn Khurram that the option of the session is established by this hadith, whether we say separation by speech or by bodies. If we say by bodies, it is clear, and similarly if we say by statements because one saying 'I sold it to you for ten' and the other saying 'no rather for twenty' is separation in speech, unlike if he said 'I bought it for ten' as they are in agreement, so the option is established for both. According to this, when separation occurs the sale is terminated, not that the option is terminated. And the apparent meaning of the hadith is the termination of the option with the contract remaining, and if it is probable then it is no longer a proof for a specific [interpretation]. And al-Bukhari narrated another version from Ibn Umar raised: 'When two men transact a sale, each of them has the option as long as they have not separated or one gives the other the option, and they are together. And if they separate after transacting and neither has left the sale, the sale is binding.' This is apparent in that the sale is dissolved by either's revocation. Al-Khattabi said: It is the clearest thing in establishing the option of the session, invalidating every interpretation that contradicts the apparent meaning of the hadith. Similarly, his statement at the end: 'And if they separate after transacting' contains clear clarification that separation is by bodies, and if its meaning were by statement the hadith would be devoid of benefit; this is from Fath al-Bari.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe on the ruling of khiyar al-majlis (option of the session) in sales. The author, al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah (being commented upon), stated that the option exists until the parties separate. The commentator clarifies that the command for witnessing in Quran 2:282 indicates the need for documentation, which would be meaningless if one party had an option, as the contract would not be final. The hadith of Hiban ibn Munqidh instructs to stipulate 'no deception', which would be unnecessary if an option already existed. However, the commentator notes that the option might be after separation, not before. Isa ibn Aban preferred the interpretation that separation is physical, based on the principle in Islamic law that physical separation causes nullification in certain transactions like currency exchange. Then the text presents a difference among later Hanafi scholars: some (like in al-Mustasfa and Fath al-Qadir) define tafarruq as verbal disagreement—the second party rejecting the offer—analogous to sects differing in creed. Others (Ghayat al-Bayan) define it as acceptance of the offer, ending the option like divorce. The consequence: on the first view, verbal rejection dissolves the sale entirely; on the second, only the option ends and the sale becomes binding. The narrator Ibn Umar understood the option of the session from the hadith and would leave his companion after buying, but his interpretation is not a proof over others. Fath al-Bari reports from Ibn Khurram that the option is established regardless of whether tafarruq is by speech or body. However, if by statements, disagreement in the counter-offer constitutes separation, whereas agreement does not. The commentator then argues that the apparent meaning of the hadith is that the option ends with the contract remaining, but since it is open to interpretation, it is not decisive. The second hadith from Bukhari explicitly states that after separation the sale is binding, which clearly indicates physical separation as per al-Khattabi. Thus, the Hanafi position is that separation is physical, and the option continues until physical separation occurs.

Key Terms
خيار المجلس (khiyar al-majlis): The right of both buyer and seller to revoke the sale as long as they remain in the session where the contract was made, before physically separating.
التفرق (al-tafarruq): Separation of the two parties; the text debates whether it means physical departure or verbal disagreement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what ends the option of the session (khiyar al-majlis)?
A) Physical separation of the parties
B) Verbal rejection of the offer by one party
C) Both physical separation and verbal rejection
D) The recitation of the offer and acceptance

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that khiyar al-majlis ends only by physical separation, not by verbal disagreement, as per the explicit hadith.

The Flexibility of Offer and Acceptance Formulas: Tense and Intention
Expansion on contract formulas: tense, intention, and imperative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when intention is required for present tense in offer/acceptance and identify which formulas are valid for concluding a sale in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A sale is valid with any wording that conveys transfer of ownership — past, present, or imperative — as long as the meaning is clear.

The source passage clarifies that the author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq did not restrict offer and acceptance to the past tense. This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on substance over form. In this lesson, we explore the conditions under which present tense and imperative can conclude a sale, including the role of intention and local custom. You will learn to distinguish valid formulas from invalid ones and apply the maxim that meaning governs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Default Rule for Past and Present Tense
Past tense concludes a sale without intention. Present tense requires intention because its common usage is for the future. Determine why the present tense needs intention despite being a reality for the present in Hanafi usul.
The need for intention arises from customary usage, not from the inherent meaning of the tense.
2
Analyze the Role of Local Custom
If the local community uses the present tense for immediate actions (like in Khwarazm), no intention is needed. Understand how 'urf can override the default rule.
Custom is a recognized source of law in Hanafi school.
3
Examine the Imperative Formula
The imperative (e.g., 'Take this') can serve as an offer if it implies the transfer of ownership. Review examples like 'Take it for such-and-such' followed by 'I took it.'
The imperative works by implication (iqtida'), not by literal wording.
4
Apply the Maxim: Meaning Over Form
The text emphasizes that what matters is the intended meaning, not the literal wording. Work through the eight situations where the imperative functions differently in sale, gift, surety, etc.
For example, in marriage, the imperative 'Marry me' becomes an offer, but in sale it does not suffice alone.

الامتناع .اهـ. وبهذا علم أن ما في الحاوي القدسي من منهما شرط في كل عقد إلا النكاح تساهل والحاصل كما في الهداية أن المعتبر في هذه العقود هو المعنى ألا ترى إلى ما قالوا لو قال وهبتك أو وهبت لك هذه الدار بألف درهم أو قال هذا العبد بثوبك هذا فرضي كان بيعاً إجماعاً، ولو قال أتبيعني عبدك هذا بألف فقال نعم فقال أخذته فهو بيع لازم فوقعت كلمة نعم إيجاباً، وكذا تقع قبولاً فيما لو قال اشتريت منك هذا بألف فقال نعم بخلاف النكاح فإنه ينعقد بالأمر كقوله زوجني لأن المساومة لا تليق به فتكون إيجاباً وقيل توكيل والواحد يتولاه بخلاف البيع إلا في الأب ومن ذكرناه معه . ذكر في النكاح أن فائدة الخلاف تظهر فيما إذا صدر And he [the author of Kanz] was absolute in [the statement of] offer and acceptance, and did not restrict them to the past tense as [stated] in al-Hidayah, because the reality is that it is not restricted by that, since it [the contract] is concluded by every two utterances that indicate the meaning of taking ownership and giving ownership, in the past or present tense, as in al-Khaniyyah. But it is concluded by [the] past [tense] without intention, and by the present with it on the correct view; this is in al-Bada'i'. And it is needed with it despite it being a reality for the present tense in our view on the correct opinion, due to its predominant use for the future, literally or figuratively; this is in al-Bada'i'. And this is what is meant by some saying that it is concluded in the future by intention. In al-Qunyah: Intention is only needed if the people of the town do not use the present tense for the present, not for promise or future. If they do (like the people of Khwarazm), then it is not needed. And he only restricted it in al-Hidayah to exclude the future only, whether imperative or present preceded by 'sa' or 'sawfa', as in al-Khaniyyah unless they convey their meaning. So if the imperative indicates the mentioned meaning, it is concluded by it—like 'Take it for such-and-such' and he says 'I took it'—it is like the past requiring a prior sale. However, the past requires a prior sale according to the [linguistic] convention, whereas 'Take' requires it by way of implication. For example, if he said 'I sold you, take this my slave for a thousand' and he said 'He is free', he becomes free and the purchase is established by implication and he becomes a taker, unlike if he said 'and he is free'. And in al-Khaniyyah: If after the offer he says 'I take it', it is not a sale. And if he says 'he is free' like 'he is free'? 'I took it' is valid. And if he said to a butcher 'Weigh out for me from this meat such-and-such for a dirham' and he did it, it is not a sale, and the commander has that meat for that dirham if he weighs it? He may refuse to take it. And if he said 'Weigh for me from a specific place', it is a sale and he may not refuse. End. And from this it is known that what is in al-Hawi al-Qudsi that the [imperative] is a condition in every contract except marriage is lenient. The conclusion, as in al-Hidayah, is that what is considered in these contracts is the meaning. Do you not see what they said: if he said 'I give you as a gift' or 'I gave you this house for a thousand dirhams' or said 'This slave for your garment' and he agreed, it is a sale by consensus. And if he said 'Will you sell me your slave for a thousand?' and he said 'Yes', then he said 'I take it', it is a binding sale, so the word 'yes' becomes an offer. Similarly, it becomes an acceptance if he said 'I bought this from you for a thousand' and he said 'Yes', unlike marriage, which is concluded by the imperative, like his saying 'Marry me', because bargaining is not befitting for it, so it becomes an offer, and it is said [to be] a deputyship, and one person undertakes it, unlike sale except for the father and those we mentioned with him. It is mentioned in [the chapter of] marriage that the benefit of the difference appears when the imperative comes from the agent. According to the first, it is acceptance and does not need the acceptance of the agent; according to the second, [it does] until he accepts. And it is decided in al-Khulasah because the agent does not have the right to deputize without permission or general authorization. And these are eight situations: one: sale and rescission—the imperative is not sufficient for the offer; marriage and khul'—they occur as an offer. Fifth: if he says to his slave 'Buy yourself from me for a thousand' and he says 'I did it', he becomes free. Sixth: in gift, if he says 'Give [it] to me' and he says 'I have given it to you', the gift is complete. Seventh: he says to the creditor 'Release me from what you have against me of debt' and he says 'I release you', release is complete. Eighth: suretyship: he says 'Become surety for the person of so-and-so to so-and-so' and he says 'I did', it is complete. If the principal is absent and later comes and authorizes the suretyship, it is valid; this is in Fath al-Qadir. And there is consideration in the formulation of suretyship; the correct [view] as in al-Khaniyyah: 'Become surety for me for my property on Zaid' or 'Become surety for me for the person of Zaid' and he says 'I did', it is complete. But in khul' there is detail: if she says 'Divorce me' and he says 'I divorce you for such-and-such', it does not occur until she accepts, unlike if she says 'Divorce me for such-and-such'. What is considered in these contracts is the meaning. Al-Ramli said: It will come in the issue of ta'ati that indication to proprietary contracts... and it may be valid.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe on the conditions of offer and acceptance (sighah) in sales. The author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq (the matn) stated the matter absolutely without restricting to past tense, unlike some formulations in al-Hidayah. The commentator clarifies that the contract is concluded by any two expressions indicating transfer of ownership (tamlik and tamalluk), whether in past or present tense, as in al-Khaniyyah. However, the past tense works without intention, while the present tense requires intention because its default usage is for the future. If the local custom uses the present tense for the present (like Khwarazm), no intention is needed. The imperative form (sighah al-amr) can also conclude a contract if it clearly indicates the meaning. For example, saying 'Take this for such-and-such' and the other takes it constitutes a sale by implication (iqtida'). The text then provides numerous examples: in sale, if the imperative is followed by explicit acceptance like 'I take it', it is valid; but if only 'I take it' without prior context, it is not. In the case of a butcher, weighing meat on command is not a sale unless a specific item is designated. The maxim is that the meaning and intent behind the words are considered, not the form. The passage then lists eight specific situations where the imperative suffices: sale and rescission do not accept the imperative as offer; marriage and khul' accept it as offer; a slave buying himself; gift; release of debt; suretyship; and khul' with detail. Al-Ramli notes that in ta'ati (hand-to-hand exchange), gestures may suffice. The overarching principle is that the substance (al-ma'na) governs, as the Hanafi school consistently holds that the objective is mutual consent and transfer of ownership.

Key Terms
إيجاب (ījāb): The statement that initiates a contract, expressing the offer of transfer of ownership.
قبول (qabūl): The statement that accepts the offer, completing the contract.
صيغة العقد (ṣīghat al-ʿaqd): The verbal formula of the contract; the text discusses its flexibility in tense and form.
اقتضاء (iqtidā'): Implication; the meaning inferred from context rather than literal wording.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, when is intention (niyyah) required for the present tense in offer or acceptance?
A) Always, because the present tense is not suitable for contracts
B) Only when the local custom does not use the present tense for the present moment
C) Never, because the present tense is a reality for the present
D) Only for the offer, not the acceptance

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, any clear expression of transfer of ownership—past, present, or imperative—concludes a valid sale; the substance of the contract matters more than its linguistic form.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Valid Expressions for the Sale Contract
Ruling statements on valid sale expressions with expansions and intra-Hanafi disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid and invalid expressions for offer and acceptance in a Hanafi sale contract and apply the rules for multiple offers and acceptances.

Core Idea
A valid sale requires explicit linguistic formulations; not every mutual agreement constitutes a valid contract.

In Hanafi fiqh, the formation of a sale contract depends on precise words. This lesson examines acceptable expressions for offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul) as detailed in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a key commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. We explore which words create a binding sale, how suspension affects validity, and how to handle multiple offers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Requirement of Immediate Expression
Analyze the passage's distinction between tunjeez (immediate) and ta'leeq (suspended). A sale suspended on an act of the heart (e.g., intention) is valid, but suspension on an external act (like payment) only validates if the act occurs in the same session.
The phrase 'إن أديت إلي ثمن هذا العبد فقد بعتك' becomes valid only if payment is made in the session.
2
List Valid Expressions for Acceptance
From the passage, identify ‘قد فعلت’, ‘نعم’, and ‘هات الثمن’ as valid acceptance according to the most correct view, with the caveat that ‘نعم’ is valid from the seller's side but disputed from the buyer's side.
The Fatwas of Samarkand hold all three as valid from either side.
3
Recognize Invalid Expressions
Note that ‘طابت نفسي’ (my soul is pleased) does not constitute a valid sale. Also, the word ‘أجزت’ (I permitted) after ‘بعت’ is invalid according to the relied-upon view (faqih Abu Ja'far and Abu al-Layth).
Abu Bakr al-Iskaf disagrees, but the preferred position rejects ‘أجزت’.
4
Understand Multiple Offers and Acceptances
If a seller makes two offers with different prices, the buyer's acceptance applies to the second offer, and the sale is with the first price. If the second offer is for a different amount (more or less), the first contract is annulled; if equal, both remain valid.
This applies to sales. In emancipation and divorce for money, both amounts are due and the second does not nullify the first.
5
Apply the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
The passage presents an ikhtilaf: Abu Bakr al-Iskaf validates ‘أجزت’ after ‘بعت’, while Faqih Abu Ja'far and Faqih Abu al-Layth reject it. The mufta bihi (operative position) is that it does not constitute a sale. Similarly, ‘نعم’ is valid from the seller's side only per one view, but the most correct view from Fatwas of Samarkand considers it valid from both sides.
The tarjih authority is al-Tatarkhaniyya and the Fatwas of Samarkand.

كتاب البيع

فقال قد فعلت؛ كذا في الصيرفية. وبهذا علم أن ما في الحاوي القدسي من أن المضي فيهما شرط في كل عقد إلا النكاح تساهل وحاصل ما في التتارخانية مما يناسب المقام أنه ينعقد بلفظ الرد وببيع معلق بفعل قلب كإن أردت فقال أردت أو إن أعجبك فقال أعجبني أو إن وافقك فقال وافقني وأما إذا قال إن أديت إلي ثمن هذا العبد فقد بعتك فإن أدى في المجلس صح، ولو قال بعت منك بألف إن شئت يوماً إلى الليل كان تنجيزاً لا تعليقاً، وبأجزت بعد قوله بعت وبقوله أقلتك هذا فقال «قبلت» على قول أبي بكر الإسكاف، وقال الفقيه أبو جعفر لا يكون بيعاً، وبه أخذ الفقيه أبو الليث. وتصح إضافة البيع إلى عضو تصح إضافة العتق إليه وما لا فلا وقد فعلت» و«نعم» و«هات الثمن قبول على الأصح. ولو قال بعني هذا بكذا فقال طابت نفسي لا ينعقد، ويصح الإيجاب بلفظ الهبة وأشركتك فيه وأدخلتك فيه إيجاب، وإذا تعدد الإيجاب فكل إيجاب بمال انصرف قبوله إلى الإيجاب الثاني ويكون بيعاً بالثمن الأول، وفي الإعتاق والطلاق على مال إذا قبل بعدهما لزمه المالان ولا يبطل الثاني الأول، وإذا تعدد الإيجاب والقبول انعقد الثاني وانفسخ الأول إن كان الثاني بأزيد من الأول أو أنقص، وإن كان مثله لم ينفسخ الأول. واختلفوا فيما إذا كان الثاني قوله : (ينعقد بلفظ الرد قال في التتارخانية : ولو قال أرد عليك هذه الأمة بخمسين ديناراً وقبل الآخر ثبت البيع. قوله : (قبول) على الأصح) أي إذا كان من طرف البائع إلا في قد فعلت فهو قبول منهما قال في التتارخانية : إذا قال لآخر بعت منك عبدي هذا بألف درهم فقال المشتري قد فعلت فهذا بيع، ولو قال نعم لا يكون بيعاً. ذكر في فتاوى أهل سمرقند أن من قال لغيره اشتريت عبدك هذا بألف درهم فقال البائع قد فعلت أو قال نعم أو قال هات الثمن صح البيع وهو الأصح اهـ. وسيذكر المؤلف في الصفحة الآتية عن الولوالجية الفرق في نعم لكن تقدم قريباً أن نعم تقع إيجاباً وقبولاً.
Chapter of Sale: He said 'I have done'; as in al-Sayrafiyya. And by this it is known that what is in al-Hawi al-Qudsi that consent is a condition in every contract except marriage is negligent. The summary of what is in al-Tatarkhaniyya relevant to the context is that it is concluded by the word 'return' and by a sale suspended on an act of the heart like 'if you intend' and he said 'I intended', or 'if it pleases you' and he said 'it pleases me', or 'if it suits you' and he said 'it suits me'. But if he said 'if you pay me the price of this slave, I have sold you', then if he pays in the session, it is valid. And if he said 'I sold you for a thousand if you wish from day to night', it is an immediate (tunjeez) not a suspended (ta'leeq). And by 'I permitted' after his saying 'I sold' and by his saying 'I have annulled you this' and he said 'I accept' according to the saying of Abu Bakr al-Iskaf, and Faqih Abu Ja'far said it is not a sale, and Faqih Abu al-Layth adopted this. And it is valid to attribute the sale to a limb to which it is valid to attribute emancipation, and not to what is not. And 'I have done', 'yes', and 'hand over the price' are acceptance according to the most correct view. And if he said 'sell me this for such' and he said 'my soul is pleased', it is not concluded. And the offer is valid by the word 'gift', and 'I have made you a partner in it', and 'I have included you in it' is an offer. And when there are multiple offers, each offer for wealth, his acceptance refers to the second offer and it is a sale with the first price. And in emancipation and divorce for money, if he accepts after them, both amounts are due and the second does not nullify the first. And when there are multiple offers and acceptances, the second is concluded and the first is annulled if the second is for more or less than the first, and if it is equal, the first is not annulled. And they differed regarding when the second... He said: (It is concluded by the word 'return'.) He said in al-Tatarkhaniyya: If he said 'I return to you this slave-girl for fifty dinars' and the other accepted, the sale is confirmed. He said: (Acceptance is the most correct.) That is, if it is from the seller's side, except in 'I have done', it is acceptance from both. He said in al-Tatarkhaniyya: If he said to another 'I have sold you my slave for a thousand dirhams' and the buyer said 'I have done', this is a sale. And if he said 'yes', it is not a sale. It is mentioned in the Fatwas of the people of Samarkand that if someone said to another 'I have bought your slave for a thousand dirhams' and the seller said 'I have done' or said 'yes' or said 'hand over the price', the sale is valid, and that is the most correct. And the author will mention later on the next page from al-Walwaljiyya the difference regarding 'yes', but it has been mentioned close by that 'yes' occurs as offer and acceptance.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a sharh (commentary) on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It begins by discussing the conditions for a valid sale contract in the Hanafi school. The text clarifies that consent (al-madi) is a condition in every contract except marriage, as some earlier scholars had stated. It then lists various expressions that constitute a valid offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul). Among these, the word 'radd' (return) can be used for sale, and a sale can be suspended on an act of the heart (e.g., intention, pleasure). However, a sale suspended on a future act like payment is only valid if the payment occurs in the same session. The phrase 'I permit' (ajaztu) after 'I sold' is valid according to some, but rejected by others. The text then discusses specific words: 'fa'ala' (I have done), 'na'am' (yes), and 'hat al-thaman' (hand over the price) are considered valid acceptance in the most correct view, though there is some disagreement, especially regarding 'na'am'. The passage also covers multiple offers: if a seller makes two offers with different prices, the buyer's acceptance goes to the second offer. In emancipation and divorce for money, if the slave or wife accepts both offers, both amounts are due. If the seller makes two offers with different prices, the second contract cancels the first unless the prices are equal. Finally, it touches on the word 'radd' being valid for sale, citing al-Tatarkhaniyya.

Key Terms
تنجيز (tunjeez): An immediate, unconditional expression of offer or acceptance, as opposed to ta'leeq (suspension).
تعليق (ta'leeq): A suspended contract conditional on a future event.
إيجاب (ijab): Offer; the first utterance in a contract indicating willingness to sell.
قبول (qabul): Acceptance; the second utterance indicating agreement to the offer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a valid acceptance from the buyer in a Hanafi sale?
A) 'أجزت' (I permitted)
B) 'طابت نفسي' (My soul is pleased)
C) 'قد فعلت' (I have done)
D) 'هات الثمن' (Hand over the price) — only if said by the seller

Key Takeaway
A valid Hanafi sale contract requires specific linguistic formulations for offer and acceptance; vague expressions like 'my soul is pleased' do not conclude the sale, and intra-Hanafi disputes exist over certain terms like 'ajaztu' and 'na'am'.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Second Sale with Different Price: Annulment of the First
Rulings on second valid sale annulling first

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that a second sale contract with a different price annuls the first valid sale, and identify the exception when the price is identical.

Core Idea
A second sale with a different price annuls the first; if equal, it is void and the first remains.

When a seller and buyer complete a sale, can the seller later make a new offer on the same item and annul the first? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the Hanafi rule: a second valid sale with a different price (more, less, or different currency) automatically annuls the first. If the price is identical, the second is futile and the first stands. This principle is based on the legal effect of changing the parties' obligations.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish offers before vs. after acceptance
If multiple offers are made before any acceptance, the buyer's acceptance goes to the last offer. If a valid sale is already concluded, a new offer after acceptance may annul the first.
The key is timing: before acceptance, it is multiple offers; after acceptance, it is a second contract.
2
Identify when a second valid sale annuls the first
According to the text, if the seller says 'I sold you this for 10' and the buyer accepts, then later (same or different session) says 'I sold you for 9' and the buyer accepts, the second is concluded and the first is annulled. The same applies if the price is more or of a different type.
The second contract must be valid (sahih) for annulment.
3
Understand the effect of equal price
If the second price is exactly the same as the first, the second is considered void because it serves no benefit; the first remains in effect.
Al-Ramli notes that the second is 'vain' (laghw) due to lack of benefit.
4
Examine the correction in the text
The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq notes that the statement 'it is a sale with the first price' should be corrected to 'with the second price', confirming that the second price governs.
This shows careful editing of earlier texts.

Source Text

قوله : (وإذا تعدد الإيجاب الخ) قال في التتارخانية : إذا قال الرجل لغيره بعتك عبدي هذا بألف درهم بعتك عبدي هذا بمائة دينار فقال المشتري قبلت ينصرف قبوله إلى الإيجاب الثاني ويكون هذا بيعاً بمائة دينار ولو قال لعبده أنت حر على ألف درهم أنت حر على مائة دينار فقال العبد قبلت لزمه المالان، ولو قال بعت منك هذا العبد بألف درهم وقبل المشتري ثم قال بعت منك بمائة دينار في المجلس أو مجلس آخر وقال المشتري اشتريت ينعقد الثاني وينفسخ الأول، وكذلك لو باعه بجنس الثمن الأول بأقل أو بأكثر نحو أن يبيعه منه بعشرة ثم باعه بتسعة أو بأحد عشر، فإن باع بعشرة لا ينعقد الثاني ويبقى الأول بحاله .اهـ. وبهذا يظهر أن قول المؤلف ويكون بيعاً بالثمن الأول صوابه بالثمن الثاني . قوله : (إن كان الثاني بأزيد من الأول أو أنقص) قال الرملي : أو كان بخلاف جنسه قوله : ( وإن كان مثله لم ينفسخ الأول قال الرملي : الظاهر في وجهه أنه لعدم فائدته وقع لغواً ومقتضاه أنه إذا كان الثاني فاسداً ينفسخ الأول لإفادته أحكاماً غير أحكام الصحيح من وجوب رد المبيع قائماً وضمان قيمته أو مثله هالكاً فتغير الأحكام فيهما يوجب انفساخ الأول تأمل.

His saying: (And when there are multiple offers etc.) He said in al-Tatarkhaniyya: If a man says to another 'I sold you my slave for a thousand dirhams, I sold you my slave for a hundred dinars' and the buyer says 'I accept', his acceptance goes to the second offer and this is a sale for a hundred dinars. And if he said to his slave 'You are free for a thousand dirhams, you are free for a hundred dinars' and the slave said 'I accept', both amounts are due. And if he said 'I sold you this slave for a thousand dirhams' and the buyer accepted, then he said 'I sold you for a hundred dinars' in the same session or another session and the buyer said 'I bought', the second is concluded and the first is annulled. Similarly, if he sold it with the same type of price for less or more, like if he sold it to him for ten then sold it for nine or eleven. If he sold for ten, the second is not concluded and the first remains as it is. And by this it becomes clear that the author's saying 'and it is a sale with the first price' should be 'with the second price'. His saying: (If the second is for more or less than the first) al-Ramli said: 'or if it is of a different type'. His saying: (And if it is equal, the first is not annulled) al-Ramli said: 'The apparent reason is that due to lack of benefit it occurs as vain, and its implication is that if the second is fasid, it annuls the first because it gives rulings different from the rulings of a valid contract, like the obligation to return the sold item in existence and the guarantee of its value or its like if destroyed. So the change of rulings in both necessitates the annulment of the first. Contemplate.' Fasid: Does it include the annulment of the first? And sulh after a second sulh is void and the first is valid. Similarly, sulh of the dowry as in... After a purchase, a sulh is void. And if the purchase is after the sulh, the purchase is valid and the sulh is void, as in Jami' al-Fusulayn. And in Furq al-Karabisi: Kafala after kafala is valid, hawala after hawala is void, marriage after the second marriage is void so he does not owe the named dowry unless he renews it for increase, as in al-Qunyah. As for ijara after ijara for the first lessee, I have not seen it. It is proper that if the period is the same and the rents are the same, the second is not valid like sale. And gift after purchase does not annul it, unlike sadaqa like rahn after it, and purchase after sadaqa annuls it. Purchase after loan is void, as in al-Qunyah. And the gift does not annul it only if it is not from the father to the child also. And waiving the price after the offer before acceptance nullifies the offer. It is also said: no, and it is a discharge. And the silence of the buyer about the price spoils the sale. And an offer without mentioning a price is not valid. And the offer is valid by the word 'ja'ala' (making) like saying 'I have made this for you for a thousand', because Muhammad mentioned that if the judge says to the creditor 'I have made this for you for your debt', it is a sale and that is correct. And it contains evidence that if he says to another 'this thing is a sale for your debt' and he accepts, it is concluded, like saying 'this slave is upon you for a thousand.' His saying: (And they differed regarding when the second is fasid etc.) He said in al-Nahr: 'The required consideration is that the first is not annulled.' Al-Ramli said: 'It is firmly held in Jami' al-Fusulayn and al-Bazzaziyya that it is annulled. In al-Hawi al-Zahidi, there is consideration against its non-annulment, where he said: there is consideration, and it is explicitly stated contrary to it. Similarly, the author of al-Dhakhira said that the second, even if fasid, includes the abrogation of the first, like if he bought a heart of silver weighing ten for ten and they received from each other, then he bought it from him for nine, it includes the annulment of the first even though the second is fasid. And al-Bazzazi and the author of Jami' al-Fusulayn reasoned that it is attached to the valid in many rulings, and Allah knows best.' His saying: (And sulh after the second sulh is void) means if the sulh is by way of forfeiture, as in al-Khulasa before the third of the sections on sales: the intended sulh is that which is a forfeiture. But if the sulh is for a consideration then they reconcile for another consideration, the second is permissible and does not annul the first like a sale. Hamawi on al-Ashbah. His saying: (As for ijara after ijara etc.) The author said in al-Ashbah: As for ijara after ijara from the first lessee, the second is an annulment of the first as in al-Bazzaziyya. It seems he saw it later, since the composition of al-Ashbah is later than this commentary. His saying: (And waiving the price after the offer etc.) He said in al-Tatarkhaniyya and in al-Fatawa: If the principal says to another 'I have sold you this for a thousand dirhams and I waive the thousand from you' and the buyer says 'I bought', the sale is valid and discharge is not permissible because the price is not yet due. And in Majmu' al-Nawazil: The sale is not valid in this case because it is in the meaning of a sale without a price. And earlier on the page it is said in al-Fatawa al-Khulasa: A man said to another 'I have sold you my slave for ten dirhams and I waive the ten from you' and the other said 'I bought', the sale is not valid. But if he sold for such of the price and the buyer accepted, then he discharged him from the price or waived it or gave it as charity, it is valid. And if he sold it and remained silent about the price, ownership is established if possession is connected, according to the saying of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. And if...
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This extended passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq continues the commentary on multiple offers and the effect of a second contract on the first. It begins by citing al-Tatarkhaniyya on the rule: if a seller makes two offers with different prices, the buyer's acceptance applies to the second offer, and the sale is for the second price. The same principle applies to emancipation and divorce on payment: if two offers are made, both are binding if accepted. If a seller completes a sale with the buyer, then in the same or a different session makes a second offer with a different price, and the buyer accepts, the second contract annuls the first — unless the price is exactly the same. The commentator notes a correction: the author's statement that it is a sale with the first price should be with the second price. Al-Ramli explains that if the second price is equal to the first, the second is void because it serves no benefit. Then the discussion moves to whether a second fasid (invalid) contract annuls the first. There is a disagreement: some say it does not, others say it does because the fasid contract still creates different legal effects. The rest of the passage provides a series of comparative rulings for various contracts: sulh, kafala, hawala, marriage, ijara, gifts, etc. For instance, a second sulh that is a forfeiture is void and the first remains; but if it is for a different consideration, the second is valid. Kafala after kafala is valid; hawala after hawala is void; marriage after marriage is void unless renewed for increase. The passage also discusses waiving the price after offer but before acceptance: whether it nullifies the offer. The correct view is that the sale is valid because the price is not yet due. Finally, it validates offer by the word 'ja'ala' (making), citing Imam Muhammad's example of a judge making property for a debt.

Key Terms
فاسد (fasid): A contract that is defective in some essential element but still produces some legal effects, such as obligation to return the object.
لاغو (laghw): Vain, futile; used here to describe a second contract with no legal benefit, thus void.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if a seller sells a slave for 10 dirhams, the buyer accepts, then the seller says 'I have sold you for 9 dirhams' and the buyer accepts?
A) The first sale is annulled and the second is valid for 9 dirhams.
B) Both sales are valid and the buyer owns the slave but owes 19 dirhams.
C) The second sale is invalid because the price is less.
D) The first sale remains valid and the second is void.

Key Takeaway
A second valid sale with a different price automatically annuls the first; if the price is identical, the second is void and the first remains.

Second Contracts and Their Effect on Prior Contracts
Listing of rules on second contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state whether a second contract (e.g., sulh, purchase, kafalah, hawalah) rescinds or invalidates the first contract in Hanafi fiqh, and identify the specific ruling for each contract type.

Core Idea
A second contract does not automatically rescind the first; the ruling depends on the contract type and order.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents a series of rulings on how a second contract affects a prior contract on the same subject matter. The Hanafi school holds that the outcome varies: some second contracts are void and leave the first intact, others are valid, and some even rescind the first. Parsing these rules helps you understand the interplay between different contract types in Islamic commercial law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract pair
For each ruling, note the first contract and the second contract (e.g., sulh after sulh, purchase after sulh).
The text often states the order explicitly.
2
Determine the order
Check which contract occurred first. The ruling often changes if the order is reversed (e.g., purchase after sulh vs. sulh after purchase).
3
Extract the ruling
State whether the second contract is valid or invalid, and whether it rescinds the first contract.
For example: 'sulh after sulh is invalid and the first remains valid'.
4
Note exceptions and analogies
Some contracts (like kafalah) have different rules. For ijarah, the text analogizes to sale. Identify the underlying principle.
5
Summarize the taxonomy
Group contracts into those that automatically invalidate the second (like hawalah, nikah), those where the second is valid (like kafalah), and those where it depends on details (like ijarah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاسداً هل يتضمن فسخ الأول والصلح بعد الصلح الثاني باطل والأول صحيح، وكذا الصلح المهر كما، بعد الشراء صلح باطل ولو كان الشراء بعد الصلح فالشراء صحيح والصلح باطل؛ كذا في جامع الفصولين. وفي فروق الكرابيسي: الكفالة بعد الكفالة صحيحة، والحوالة بعد الحوالة باطلة، والنكاح بعد النكاح الثاني باطل فلا يلزمه المهر المسمى فيه إلا إذا جدده للزيادة في القنية، وأما الإجارة بعد الإجارة للمستأجر الأول فلم أرها، وينبغي أن المدة إذا اتحدت فيهما واتحد الأجران لا تصح الثانية كالبيع، وأما الهبة بعد الشراء فلا تفسخه دون الصدقة كالرهن بعده والشراء بعد الصدقة يفسخها، والشراء بعد القرض باطل؛ كذا في القنية. والهبة إنما لم تفسخه إذا لم يكن للولد منهما أيضاً وهبة الثمن بعد الإيجاب قبل القبول مبطل للإيجاب. وقيل: لا ويكون إبراء وسكوت المشتري عن الثمن مفسد للبيع وإيجاب البيع بلا ثمن نفياً غير صحيح. ويصح الإيجاب بلفظ الجعل كقوله جعلت لك هذا بألف لما ذكره محمد أن القاضي إذا قال للدائن جعلت لك هذا بدينك كان بيعاً وهو الصحيح، من وفيه دليل على أنه لو قال لغيره هذا الشي بيع بدينك فقبل انعقد كقوله هذا العبد عليك بألف
Whether a [second contract that is] invalid implies rescission of the first: sulh after sulh is invalid and the first is valid. Likewise, sulh of the dower as... Sulh after purchase is invalid; if purchase is after sulh, the purchase is valid and the sulh is invalid. So in Jami' al-Fusulayn. In Furuq al-Karabisi: Kafalah after kafalah is valid, hawalah after hawalah is invalid, nikah after nikah is invalid and the dower named therein is not obligatory unless it was renewed for increase (in al-Qunyah). As for ijarah after ijarah for the first lessee, I have not seen it and it should be that if the period is the same in both and the two rents are same, the second is invalid like sale. Hiba after purchase does not rescind it, unlike sadaqah; likewise rahn after it; purchase after sadaqah rescinds it; purchase after qard is invalid. So in al-Qunyah. Hiba does not rescind it if it is not from a parent to a child also. Hiba of the price after offer and before acceptance invalidates the offer. Some say it does not and it is a discharge. The buyer's silence about the price spoils the sale. An offer of sale without mentioning a price is invalid. An offer is valid with the word 'ja'altu' such as one says 'I made this yours for a thousand' because Muhammad mentioned that if the judge says to the creditor 'I made this yours in place of your debt' it is a sale and that is correct. It contains evidence that if one says to another 'this thing is a sale in place of your debt' and he accepts, it is concluded like his saying 'this slave is owed to you for a thousand dirhams'.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
هذا النص من متن كنز الدقائق يسرد أحكاماً متعددة تتعلق بتأثير العقد الثاني في العقد الأول عند اتحاد المحل، وبعض صيغ الإيجاب. المسألة الأولى: إذا وقع عقد ثان فاسد على نفس محل العقد الأول، فهل يعد ذلك فسخاً للأول؟ النص يذكر تفصيلاً: الصلح بعد الصلح (إذا كان صلح إسقاط) الثاني باطل والأول صحيح. أما إذا كان صلحاً بعوض، فالثاني جائز ولا يفسخ الأول كالبيع. ثم يذكر النص من فروق الكرابيسي أحكاماً أخرى: الكفالة بعد الكفالة صحيحة، والحوالة بعد الحوالة باطلة، والنكاح بعد النكاح الثاني باطل ولا يلزم المهر إلا إذا جدد للزيادة. الإجارة بعد الإجارة للمستأجر الأول لم يرد فيها نص، وينبغي أن تبطل إن اتحدت المدة والأجر. الهبة بعد الشراء لا تفسخه، بخلاف الصدقة. الشراء بعد الصدقة يفسخها، والشراء بعد القرض باطل. ثم ينتقل إلى هبة الثمن بعد الإيجاب وقبل القبول: هي مبطلة للإيجاب عند البعض، وعند آخرين لا تبطل وتكون إبراءً. سكوت المشتري عن الثمن يفسد البيع. إيجاب البيع بلا ثمن غير صحيح. ويصح الإيجاب بلفظ الجعل، كقول القاضي للدائن: جعلت لك هذا بدينك، فيكون بيعاً صحيحاً.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): عقد يرفع النزاع بإسقاط بعض الحق أو بعوض، ويذكر النص نوعين: صلح إسقاط وصلح بعوض.
حوالة (hawalah): تحويل الدين من ذمة المحيل إلى ذمة المحال عليه.
إجارة (ijarah): عقد على منفعة بعوض.
هبة (hibah): تمليك بلا عوض.
صدقة (sadaqah): تمليك بلا عوض بنية القربة.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling when a person makes a kafalah (guarantee) after a previous kafalah?
A) The second kafalah is invalid and the first stands
B) The second kafalah is valid
C) Both are invalid
D) The first kafalah is rescinded

Key Takeaway
The validity and effect of a second contract on the same subject vary by contract type; always check the specific ruling from transmitted texts.

Offer (Ijab) Rulings: Timing, Silence, and Formulations
Rules on offer formation and validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on when an offer is invalidated by actions like giving away the price, the effect of buyer's silence, the requirement of a price, and the validity of offers using the word 'ja'altu'.

Core Idea
An offer of sale is binding until acceptance or conditional rescission; silence about price or using invalid wording spoils it.

The latter part of the Kanz al-Daqa'iq passage focuses on rules surrounding the offer (ijab) in a sale contract. It covers three issues: the effect of the seller giving away the price after the offer but before acceptance, the buyer's silence regarding the price, the necessity of mentioning a price, and the validity of offers using alternative wording like 'ja'altu'. These rules ensure clarity and enforceability in contractual formation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the offer-related issues
The text presents four sub-topics: (1) hiba of the price after offer, (2) silence of the buyer about price, (3) offer without price, (4) offer using 'ja'altu'.
2
Analyse the hiba of price
Understand that giving away the price after offer but before acceptance is disputed: some say it invalidates the offer, others say it is a discharge (ibra) not affecting the offer.
The text says: 'وهبة الثمن بعد الإيجاب قبل القبول مبطل للإيجاب. وقيل: لا ويكون إبراء'.
3
Analyse buyer's silence
If the buyer remains silent about the price (i.e., does not specify or agree), the sale is spoilt (فسد).
The text: 'وسكوت المشتري عن الثمن مفسد للبيع'.
4
Analyse offer without price
An offer of sale without mentioning a price is invalid.
The text: 'وإيجاب البيع بلا ثمن نفياً غير صحيح'.
5
Analyse 'ja'altu' wording
An offer using the word 'ja'altu' (I made) is valid if it includes a price; it is treated as a sale by analogy to a judge saying 'I made this yours for your debt'.
The text: 'ويصح الإيجاب بلفظ الجعل كقوله جعلت لك هذا بألف'.

Commentary
هذا النص من متن كنز الدقائق يسرد أحكاماً متعددة تتعلق بتأثير العقد الثاني في العقد الأول عند اتحاد المحل، وبعض صيغ الإيجاب. المسألة الأولى: إذا وقع عقد ثان فاسد على نفس محل العقد الأول، فهل يعد ذلك فسخاً للأول؟ النص يذكر تفصيلاً: الصلح بعد الصلح (إذا كان صلح إسقاط) الثاني باطل والأول صحيح. أما إذا كان صلحاً بعوض، فالثاني جائز ولا يفسخ الأول كالبيع. ثم يذكر النص من فروق الكرابيسي أحكاماً أخرى: الكفالة بعد الكفالة صحيحة، والحوالة بعد الحوالة باطلة، والنكاح بعد النكاح الثاني باطل ولا يلزم المهر إلا إذا جدد للزيادة. الإجارة بعد الإجارة للمستأجر الأول لم يرد فيها نص، وينبغي أن تبطل إن اتحدت المدة والأجر. الهبة بعد الشراء لا تفسخه، بخلاف الصدقة. الشراء بعد الصدقة يفسخها، والشراء بعد القرض باطل. ثم ينتقل إلى هبة الثمن بعد الإيجاب وقبل القبول: هي مبطلة للإيجاب عند البعض، وعند آخرين لا تبطل وتكون إبراءً. سكوت المشتري عن الثمن يفسد البيع. إيجاب البيع بلا ثمن غير صحيح. ويصح الإيجاب بلفظ الجعل، كقول القاضي للدائن: جعلت لك هذا بدينك، فيكون بيعاً صحيحاً.

Key Terms
إيجاب (ijab): الإيجاب هو التعبير عن الإرادة بإنشاء العقد، وهو أول ركن في البيع.
ثمن (thaman): الثمن هو العوض المالي في عقد البيع.
جعل (ja'l): لفظ يستعمل في الإيجاب بمعنى التمليك.
إبراء (ibra): إسقاط الدين أو الحق المالي.
